Monday's Markets
Stocks Sputter Amid Trading Torpor --- Six-Session Winning Streak for Dow, S&P 500 Snapped as the Urge to Buy or Sell Remains Scarce
By Dan Strumpf
907 words
24 June 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Stocks stalled after their latest push to record highs, with small losses ending a six-session winning streak for both the Dow Jones Industrial Average and the S&P 500 index.

News of a handful of corporate deals provided the only significant driver of activity for traders in an otherwise uneventful session for broad market benchmarks.

The Dow industrials slipped 9.82 points, or 0.1%, to 16937.26. The S&P 500 index eased 0.26 to 1962.61.

"We had some merger events and some good economic data, but it hasn't been enough to get the market to care," said Michael Antonelli, equity sales trader at brokerage Robert W. Baird. "We're just kind of treading water."

The Nasdaq Composite Index added 0.64 point to 4368.68. It is up 4.6% in 2014. The Russell 2000 index of small-company stocks declined 0.3%; it is up 1.8% for the year to date at 1184.95.

As has been in the case for many recent sessions, trading volumes were low. Monday marked the ninth-slowest session of the year, with 5.1 billion shares changing hands, below the year-to-date average of 6.4 billion shares a day.

At the same time, broad market benchmarks have been confined to small moves. Monday was the 46th consecutive session in which the S&P 500 closed with a move higher or lower of less than a point.

Despite the modest moves, the S&P 500 has posted 22 records this year and is up 6.2% for 2014. The Dow is up 2.2% this year.

Deal activity captured much of investors' attention Monday. Shares of Micros Systems Inc. rose $2.21, or 3.4%, to $67.98 after The Wall Street Journal reported that Oracle Corp. is close to sealing a deal to buy the software company for about $5 billion. Oracle edged up 28 cents, or 0.7%, to 41.10.

Integrys Energy Group Inc. surged 7.40, or 12.1%, to 68.35 after the company agreed to be acquired by Wisconsin Energy Corp. for $5.71 billion in cash and stock. Wisconsin Energy shares fell 1.62, or 3.5%, to 45.27.

Lululemon Athletica Inc. rallied 1.02, or 2.5%, to 41.25 after the Journal reported that the yoga-gear maker's founder, Dennis "Chip" Wilson, is working with bankers at Goldman Sachs Group Inc. to find a way to gain more influence over the company's operations.

Central Garden & Pet Co. climbed 58 cents, or 6.4%, to 9.58 after Harbinger Group Inc. said it has offered to buy the company for about $505 million, or $1.1 billion including debt. Harbinger shares rose 15 cents, or 1.2%, to 13.11.

Dan Veru, chief investment officer at Palisade Capital Management, which manages $5 billion, said he has been encouraged by the recent pickup in merger activity, which is a sign that corporations feel more comfortable putting cash to use.

"There's a lot of pent-up demand for companies to deploy cash and put it into a productive asset like a company," Mr. Veru said. He said he has benefited by owning some stocks that have been acquisition targets, and expects the rise in deal making to power the market higher this year.

On the economic news front, existing home sales increased 4.9% in May to a higher than expected annual rate of 4.89 million, according to the National Association of Realtors.

In the government-bond market, the yield on the 10-year Treasury note eased slightly to 2.622% from 2.625% late Friday.

Gold futures gained 0.1% to $1318 an ounce, its fourth session in a row of gains. Crude-oil futures fell 0.6% to $106.17 a barrel. The dollar edged higher against the euro but lost some ground against the yen.

In Europe, Markit's preliminary June purchasing managers index for the euro zone, which measures activity across both the manufacturing and services sectors, fell to 52.8 from 53.5 in May, missing expectations of 53.3. Readings above 50 signal expansion. The main weak spot was France, where activity contracted for a second consecutive month, and at a quickening pace.

European markets declined following the disappointing euro-zone data. The Stoxx Europe 600 fell 0.5%, after rising last week to post the 12th weekly gain in 14 weeks.

France's CAC-40 index lost 0.6%, Germany's DAX 30 gave up 0.7% and the U.K.'s FTSE 100 fell 0.4%.

Early Tuesday in Asia, Japanese stocks moved lower, weighed by a stronger yen, while Hong Kong bounced back from a sharp fall in the previous session. The Nikkei was down 0.5% as the dollar failed to hold on to the 102 yen mark. The Hang Seng Index was up 0.3% after a 1.7% dive Monday, its sharpest one-day decline in three months, on weakened hopes for stimulus. The Shanghai composite was down 0.1% and South Korea's Kospi was up 0.5%.

---

Daniel Inman contributed to this article.

U.S. News: Drop in Short Sales Trims House Inventory
By Joe Light
805 words
23 June 2014
The Wall Street Journal (Europe Edition)
WSJE
6
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved

Short sales of underwater homes have fallen sharply amid the expiration of a key tax break, a situation that could slow the housing recovery and further limits an already thin supply of houses for sale.

Such sales, where owners sell their homes for a price below the balance on the mortgage, reduce the number of houses that end up in foreclosure. In most cases for the sale to proceed, lenders must approve the purchase and agree to forgive the unpaid portion of the mortgage owed by the homeowner.

Short sales had been especially common in recent years in hard-hit states like Florida, Michigan and Nevada, where most homes remain valued at prices that are substantially lower than during the housing boom. In March, about 5% of home purchases nationwide -- some 18,258 -- were short sales, according to mortgage-technology-and-services firm Black Knight Financial Services. That was down from 6.4% in February and off sharply from the 19.7%, or 51,909, that were short sales in January 2012.

This year's drop can be traced in part to the December expiration of the Mortgage Forgiveness Debt Relief Act, which Congress originally passed in 2007. Before the act, when a home was sold through a short sale and the lender forgave a portion of the mortgage debt, the seller would typically be required to pay income taxes on the amount forgiven. The act made the forgiven debt tax-free, which paved the way for short sales and helped speed the housing recovery.

"It's a big concern," said Veronica Malolos, a real-estate broker in Kissimmee, Fla. Ms. Malolos said some underwater sellers delisted their properties in January and February after learning that the tax provision wouldn't be extended.

Ms. Malolos's clients Javier and Mayra Gonzalez in Kissimmee said they tried to sell their home last summer after Mr. Gonzalez found a new job but took it off the market in the new year. The couple received offers of about $145,000 on the home, on which they owe about $206,000, including debt from a home-equity line of credit, but their bank wouldn't accept them.

Because the mortgage act wasn't extended, the couple estimate they would owe about $15,000 in additional income taxes based on the $61,000 difference, something they say they couldn't afford.

This year, the couple's bank began foreclosure proceedings on their home, but they said they are working things out with the bank and are staying put, even though Mr. Gonzalez now has a commute of about an hour and 40 minutes each way to his new job in Vero Beach.

Short sales also have tumbled because of rising home prices, which pushed many homes back above water or closer to it. The median existing-home price nationwide was $201,700 in April, 5.2% higher than in April 2013, according to the National Association of Realtors . In the first quarter, about 19% of homes were worth less than their mortgage, according to the real-estate-information website Zillow, down from 31% a year ago.

With would-be short sellers on the sidelines, the housing market may take longer to work through remaining underwater homes, restricting the already tight home inventory on the market. If some potential short sellers decide to go through a foreclosure instead, that could cause higher losses for mortgage-bond investors, or companies that guarantee payment of mortgages, which tend to recover less in a foreclosure because of the costs of carrying a home.

The Senate Finance Committee in April passed a bill to extend the forgiveness provision, along with many other tax breaks that had expired. But the bill stalled in May after Senate Majority Leader Harry Reid and Republicans couldn't agree on how to amend the measure.

Now some analysts don't expect Congress to move on a bill until December, after the midterm elections. Any extension would likely come as part of a wider package of tax-break extensions. "This is trapped, and there's little hope of prying it loose," said Jaret Seiberg, financial-policy analyst for Guggenheim Securities LLC,

In the meantime, real-estate agents say sellers are loath to consider short sales on homes, even when facing foreclosure as the alternative. That is a problem not just for troubled homeowners, but also for banks and mortgage giants Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac , which typically lose more money when homes are sold through a foreclosure than through a short sale.

"There are still millions of homes underwater, but short sales have fallen off considerably," said Mark Zandi, chief economist at Moody's Analytics. "It's gumming up the system" and could be limiting home-buyer activity.

US
Existing Home Sales Rise Strongly in May; 4.9% Gain Shows Buyers Are Adjusting to Modestly Higher Interest Rates
By Eric Morath
580 words
23 June 2014
09:10
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

WASHINGTON—Sales of previously-owned homes rose strongly in May but remain shy of last year's pace, a sign the housing market has tentatively regained its footing this spring.

Sales of existing homes increased 4.9% the past month to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 4.89 million homes, the National Association of Realtors said Monday. The pace was the highest since October, the last month annualized sales topped the five-million mark.

Coupled with an improvement in April, the May figure points to a housing market that has bounced back from a dismal winter and shows buyers are adjusting to modestly higher interest rates compared with the spring of 2013.

Still, May sales were down 5% from the year-earlier level and the recent rebound could be slowed if interest rates on mortgage loans begin to increase, as many economist project. Because of the sluggish start to 2014, the Realtors group expects a 3% reduction in sales this year compared with 2013's total of 5.09 million.

"It would be risky to assume this gain marks the start of a sustainable rebound," said Ian Shepherdson, chief economist at Pantheon Macroeconomics. The recent sales gains are largely "a reversal of the winter hit," and other reports do "not support the idea that demand is recovering," he said.

After a strong start last year, the pace of home sales began to ease in August before perking up this spring.

The roller coaster coincides with interest rates that jumped a percentage point in the spring of 2013 but have moderated recently, touching a six-month low in May. The average interest rate on a 30-year fixed-rate mortgage stood at 4.17% last week, according to Freddie Mac.

Lower mortgage rates make home purchases more affordable for borrowers. But Realtors economist Lawrence Yun said he expects rates to start moving back up soon, reaching 5% by the first quarter of 2015.

Rates have edged lower this year even as the Federal Reserve scaled back its bond-buying program designed to push down long-term interest rates. That program is expected to end later this year, and then the Fed will contemplate increasing short-term rates from near zero.

A rate increase, which most Fed officials say will happen next year, likely would put upward pressure on mortgage rates.

Monday's report showed the median sale price for a home in May was up 5.1% from a year earlier to $213,400. That was the slowest annual gain since March 2012.

The inventory of homes available for sale rose 6% from a year earlier. At the current pace, it would take 5.6 months to exhaust the supply, just below a typical level. The increased inventory drove May's strong sales pace, said IHS Global Insight U.S. economist Stephanie Karol.

A larger inventory of homes could set up a virtuous cycle where the better selection draws buyers to the market, which sustains price gains, which then entice additional sellers to list. One weak link in that chain, however, could be first-time buyers. The share of first-time buyers fell in May even as sales rose.

"The market can only retain its strength for so long if move-up buyers cannot find a first-time buyer to purchase their starter homes," Ms. Karol said.

Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

Markets
Asian Morning Briefing: U.S. Stocks Pause
1894 words
23 June 2014
13:36
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

MARKETS AT A GLANCE

(Data as of approximately 5 p.m. ET)

SNAPSHOT

Stocks finished little changed in quiet trading, as investors paused to assess the market's recent gains. Treasurys finished flat to slightly higher on mixed global data. Upbeat Chinese manufacturing data boosted commodity-based currencies against the dollar. Oil futures ended lower as worries about Iraq's oil production eased. Gold settled slightly higher as investors paused after last week's rally.

OPENING CALL

The Asian data calendar will remain quiet the next few days now that HSBC's preliminary data on the Chinese manufacturing sector is out of the way. The main event in the region Tuesday is the release of Japanese Prime Minister Shinzo Abe's second growth strategy package. Despite widespread skepticism from foreign investors, the program Mr. Abe will unveil has the potential to alter Japanese business practices and create new efficiencies in the economy.

EQUITIES

Stocks stalled in their push for fresh record high Monday, as investors paused to assess the market's recent gains and attention shifted to a spate of corporate deal news.

News of a handful of corporate tie-ups drove buying and selling in select stocks, according to Wall Street trading desks, but overall trading activity was subdued, extending a lull in stock trading that has lingered since early spring. Volume was the ninth lowest of the year.

"We had some merger events and some good economic data, but it hasn't been enough to get the market to care," said Michael Antonelli, an equity sales trader at brokerage Robert W. Baird. "We're just kind of treading water."

Many investors continued to sound an upbeat note on the outlook for stocks. Dan Veru, chief investment officer at Palisade Capital Management, which manages $5 billion, said he has been encouraged by the recent pickup in merger activity, which is a sign that corporations feel more comfortable putting cash to use.

"There's a lot of pent-up demand for companies to deploy cash and put it into a productive asset like a company," Mr. Veru said. He said he has benefited by owning some stocks that have been acquisition targets and expects the rise in deal-making to power the market higher this year.

Investors were also cheered by an upbeat report on the housing market. Existing home sales increased 4.9% in May to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 4.89 million, according to the National Association of Realtors. Economists surveyed by The Wall Street Journal had forecast a rate of 4.75 million for the month.

Deal activity captured much of investors' attention. Shares of Micros Systems rose 3.4% after The Wall Street Journal reported that Oracle is close to sealing a deal to buy the software company for about $5 billion. Oracle edged up 0.7%.

Integrys Energy Group surged 12.1% after the company agreed to be acquired by Wisconsin Energy Corp. for $5.71 billion in cash and stock. Wisconsin Energy shares fell 3.5%.

Lululemon Athletica rallied 2.5% after The Wall Street Journal reported the yoga-gear maker's founder, Dennis "Chip" Wilson, is working with bankers at Goldman Sachs to find a way to gain more influence over the company's operations.

Central Garden & Pet climbed 6.4% after Harbinger Group said it has offered to buy the company for about $505 million, or $1.1 billion including debt. Harbinger shares rose 1.2%.

In Asian trading Monday, markets were mixed as investors assessed upbeat manufacturing data out of China. The Shanghai Composite Index slipped 0.1% on expectations that the data might urge policy makers to hold back on stimulus measures. Japan's Nikkei Stock Average rose 0.1% to close at a near five-month high.

FOREX

Commodity-based currencies held onto most gains made against the dollar and the yen after a surprise jump in factory activity for China, the world's second-largest economy, pointed to increased demand for the natural resources produced in Australia and New Zealand.

The Chinese HSBC preliminary manufacturing Purchasing Managers' Index showed fresh signs of strength in June. The index came in at 50.8, up from 49.4 in May. It was the first time this year that the index showed expansion from the previous month. A reading below 50 indicates contraction.

The Australian and Canadian dollars also benefited from an improved trading backdrop. The conclusion of last week's Federal Open Market Committee meeting removed a degree of risk from the marketplace, said Camilla Sutton, chief currency strategist at Scotiabank.

"We've gotten the big risk hurdles out of the way last week," Ms. Sutton said. "That has provided a stable backdrop [for carry-trade currencies]. Higher-yielding currencies, like the Australian dollar, have a better outlook, because in the low-volatility environment, they do well."

BONDS

Treasury bonds eked out slim gains as softening data out of the euro zone bolstered hopes that major central banks would keep interest rates low to support growth.

Prices were kept in check by upbeat reports on the U.S. housing market and China's manufacturing industry. Bond investors also face $107 billion in new U.S. government debt supply in the next three sessions.

Trading was lackluster, which might have exaggerated part of the price swings. About $147 billion worth of Treasury bonds changed hands as of 2 p.m. EDT, said Adrian Miller, a fixed-income strategist at GMP Securities, It marks the lowest daily volume in a month.

A preliminary June purchasing managers index for the euro zone fell to a six-month low of 52.8. The main weak spot was France, where activity contracted for a second straight month, and at a quickening pace.

"Weak data from Europe support the case that additional measures remain on the table for accommodation, keeping bond yields lower," said Mary Ann Hurley, vice president of trading in Seattle at D.A. Davidson & Co. Geopolitical tensions in Iraq also encouraged some investors to seek safety in Treasury bonds, she added.

COMMODITIES

Oil futures slipped as Iraq's oil production continued to be untouched by ongoing violence in the country.

"The market is kind of catching its breath after last week's strong rally," said Gene McGillian, broker and analyst at Tradition Energy in Stamford, Conn. "Without a supply disruption, I think the majority of the political risk has been priced in."

The biggest danger to Iraq's oil production is not that current output will be disrupted, but that continued uncertainty will prompt international oil companies to retreat from Iraq, lowering future production growth, said Harry Tchilinguirian, head of commodity markets strategy at BNP Paribas.

"At this juncture, there's no particular reason for oil prices to move that much higher or that much lower from the gains they've already made," Mr. Tchilinguirian said. "You would need a new event to provoke that kind of change."

Gold prices shuffled between gains and losses, eventually settling slightly higher, as investors tried to gauge how the precious metal will trade after last week's rally.

TODAY'S HEADLINES

Oracle Agrees to Buy Micros Systems for $5.3 Billion

Oracle agreed to buy Micros Systems for about $5.3 billion including items such as options, and $4.6 billion net of Micros's cash. The deal is the biggest for Oracle since its $7.4 billion acquisition of Sun Microsystems in 2010.

U.S. Existing Home Sales Up 4.9% in May

Sales of previously owned homes exceeded economists' expectations in May, data from the National Association of Realtors show, a sign the housing market strengthened during the important spring buying season.

Iraqi Leader Commits to Steps on New Government

Secretary of State John Kerry said he received a commitment from Iraqi Prime Minister Nouri al-Maliki to begin the process of forming the next national government in Baghdad by July 1. Meanwhile, Sunni rebels attacked a convoy near Baghdad, killing at least 81 Iraqis.

Ukraine Separatists to Join Cease-Fire

Separatist leaders in Ukraine agreed to join a government-declared cease fire as a first step toward peace talks, marking a potential breakthrough in a two-month-old rebellion in the country's east. Separately, U.S. President Obama spoke with Russian President Putin, urging steps to de-escalate the crisis.

South African Miners End Strike

South Africa's main platinum mining union said that its members agreed to a wage settlement that will end a five-month strike, the country's longest, that has left the industry's labor ties in tatters.

Rio Tinto's Mongolia Mine Hits Another Snag

One of Rio Tintos largest projects hit another snag after the company running the $6.2 billion Oyu Tolgoi copper and gold mine in Mongolia was handed a new audit claiming unpaid taxes and penalties.

Argentina to Submit Terms for Debt Dispute Talks

The Argentine government has asked U.S. District Judge Thomas Griesa to temporarily suspend his ruling in a dispute between Argentina and creditors that could see the country default for a second time in 13 years.

Honda, Nissan, Mazda Recall Nearly 3 Million Vehicles

Three Japanese car makers said they were recalling nearly 3 million more cars equipped with potentially faulty air bags from parts supplier Takata, bringing the total number of vehicles affected by the bags to nearly 10 million.

Chinese Firm Resolves Paramount Dispute About 'Transformers' Movie

A Chinese company said it resolved its dispute with Paramount Pictures over the film "Transformers: Age of Extinction" just days after it demanded that its logos and properties be removed from the movie ahead of its scheduled world-wide opening.

Tiger Management Backing New Asia-Focused Fund

Julian Robertson's Tiger Management is said to be backing startup hedge-fund Kang Global Investors with as much as $50 million for its launch at the start of July.

Markets
U.S. Government Bonds Eke Out Gains on European Data; Upbeat Reports on U.S. Housing, Chinese Manufacturing Keep Prices in Check
By Min Zeng
611 words
23 June 2014
11:36
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

Treasury bonds eked out slim gains on Monday as softening data out of the euro zone bolstered hopes that major central banks would keep interest rates low to support growth.

Prices were kept in check by upbeat reports on the U.S. housing market and China's manufacturing industry. Bond investors also face $107 billion in new U.S. government debt supply in the next three sessions.

In late-afternoon trade, the benchmark 10-year Treasury note was 1/32 higher, yielding 2.620%, according to Tradeweb. The 30-year bond was 4/32 higher, yielding 3.446%. Bond yields fall when their prices rise.

Trading was lackluster Monday, which might have exaggerated part of the price swings. About $147 billion worth of Treasury bonds changed hands as of 2 p.m. EDT, said Adrian Miller, a fixed-income strategist at GMP Securities LLC. It marks the lowest daily volume in a month.

The 10-year Treasury yield has dropped from 3% at the start of January. Bond prices have been boosted by the uneven pace of global economic growth, geopolitical risk in some developing countries and the record-low interest-rate policies of major central banks.

A preliminary June purchasing managers index for the euro zone—which measures activity across the manufacturing and services sectors—fell to a six-month low of 52.8. The main weak spot was France, where activity contracted for a second straight month, and at a quickening pace.

"Weak data from Europe support the case that additional measures remain on the table for accommodation, keeping bond yields lower," said Mary Ann Hurley, vice president of trading in Seattle at D.A. Davidson & Co. Geopolitical tensions in Iraq also encouraged some investors to seek safety in Treasury bonds, she added.

The euro-zone data sent bond yields lower broadly in the region. The yield on the 10-year government bond in Spain fell to 2.696% while that on the 10-year government bond in Italy slid to 2.798%.

U.S. Treasury bonds offer superior yields compared with Germany, the benchmark for the euro zone's sovereign debt markets, enticing investors who are seeking relative value. The 10-year German government bond's yield fell to 1.324% Monday.

Reports out of China and the U.S. offered some optimism.

A gauge of China's manufacturing sector hit a seven-month high, which reduced fears about a sharp slowdown in the world's second-largest economy. Existing home sales in the U.S. increased 4.9% in May, and the pace was faster than economists forecast.

Major central banks have cut interest rates to near zero following the 2008 financial crisis. The European Central Bank earlier this month even cut one of its key interest rates below zero, aiming to spur banks to lend to consumers and businesses.

ECB President Mario Draghi suggested earlier in June that the bank hasn't finished stimulating the economy. The International Monetary Fund has called for the ECB to launch a large-scale asset purchase program, including buying of government bonds.

Federal Reserve Chairwoman Janet Yellen said last week the U.S. central bank may keep interest rates low for a considerable period even as debate has grown on signs that inflation is picking up from very low levels.

Ms. Yellen described recent inflation data as being on the "high side," but she stressed that those figures are "noisy" and that inflation is evolving in line with the central bank's expectations.

Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

U.S. News: Drop in Short Sales Trims House Inventory
By Joe Light

"This is trapped, and there's little hope of prying it loose," said Jaret Seiberg, financial-policy analyst for Guggenheim Securities LLC,

That is a problem not just for troubled homeowners, but also for banks and mortgage giants Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac , which typically lose more money when homes are sold through a foreclosure than through a short sale. In the first quarter, for example, Freddie Mac said that in short sales, it recovered 68.4 cents for every dollar of unpaid principal. In foreclosures, Freddie recovered 64.4 cents for every dollar.

"There are still millions of homes underwater, but short sales have fallen off considerably," said Mark Zandi, chief economist at Moody's Analytics. "It's gumming up the system" and could be limiting home-buyer activity.

Economy

By Joe Light

20 June 2014
17:43
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO

Short sales of underwater homes have fallen sharply amid the expiration of a key tax break, a situation that could slow the housing recovery and further limits an already thin supply of houses for sale.

Such sales, where owners sell their homes for a price below the balance on the mortgage, reduce the number of houses that end up in foreclosure. In most cases for the sale to proceed, lenders must approve the purchase and agree to forgive the unpaid portion of the mortgage owed by the homeowner.

Short sales had been especially common in recent years in hard-hit states like Florida, Michigan and Nevada, where most homes remain valued at prices that are substantially lower than during the housing boom. In March, about 5% of home purchases nationwide—some 18,258—were short sales, according to mortgage-technology-and-services firm Black Knight Financial Services. That was down from 6.4% in February and off sharply from the 19.7%, or 51,909, that were short sales in January 2012.

This year's drop can be traced in part to the December expiration of the Mortgage Forgiveness Debt Relief Act, which Congress originally passed in 2007. Before the act, when a home was sold through a short sale and the lender forgave a portion of the mortgage debt, the seller would typically be required to pay income taxes on the amount forgiven. The act made the forgiven debt tax-free, which paved the way for short sales and helped speed the housing recovery.

"It's a big concern," said Veronica Malolos, a real-estate broker in Kissimmee, Fla. Ms. Malolos said some underwater sellers delisted their properties in January and February after learning that the tax provision wouldn't be extended.

Ms. Malolos's clients Javier and Mayra Gonzalez in Kissimmee said they tried to sell their home last summer after Mr. Gonzalez found a new job but took it off the market in the new year. The couple received offers of about $145,000 on the home, on which they owe about $206,000, including debt from a home-equity line of credit, but their bank wouldn't accept them.

Because the mortgage act wasn't extended, the couple estimate they would owe about $15,000 in additional income taxes based on the $61,000 difference, something they say they couldn't afford.

This year, the couple's bank began foreclosure proceedings on their home, but they said they are working things out with the bank and are staying put, even though Mr. Gonzalez now has a commute of about an hour and 40 minutes each way to his new job in Vero Beach.

Short sales also have tumbled because of rising home prices, which pushed many homes back above water or closer to it. The median existing-home price nationwide was $201,700 in April, 5.2% higher than in April 2013, according to the National Association of Realtors . In the first quarter, about 19% of homes were worth less than their mortgage, according to the real-estate-information website Zillow, down from 31% a year ago.

With would-be short sellers on the sidelines, the housing market may take longer to work through remaining underwater homes, restricting the already tight home inventory on the market. If some potential short sellers decide to go through a foreclosure instead, that could cause higher losses for mortgage-bond investors, or companies that guarantee payment of mortgages, which tend to recover less in a foreclosure because of the costs of carrying a home.

The Senate Finance Committee in April passed a bill to extend the forgiveness provision, along with many other tax breaks that had expired. But the bill stalled in May after Senate Majority Leader Harry Reid and Republicans couldn't agree on how to amend the measure.

Now some analysts don't expect Congress to move on a bill until December, after the midterm elections. Any extension would likely come as part of a wider package of tax-break extensions. "This is trapped, and there's little hope of prying it loose," said Jaret Seiberg, financial-policy analyst for Guggenheim Securities LLC,

In the meantime, real-estate agents say sellers are loath to consider short sales on homes, even when facing foreclosure as the alternative. That is a problem not just for troubled homeowners, but also for banks and mortgage giants Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac , which typically lose more money when homes are sold through a foreclosure than through a short sale. In the first quarter, for example, Freddie Mac said that in short sales, it recovered 68.4 cents for every dollar of unpaid principal. In foreclosures, Freddie recovered 64.4 cents for every dollar.

"There are still millions of homes underwater, but short sales have fallen off considerably," said Mark Zandi, chief economist at Moody's Analytics. "It's gumming up the system" and could be limiting home-buyer activity.

U.S. News: New-Home Building Shows Sluggishness
By Jonathan House
592 words
18 June 2014
The Wall Street Journal Europe
WSJE
9
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

WASHINGTON -- A gauge of new-home construction fell in May after several months of gains, the latest sign of the housing sector's uneven recovery.

Housing starts fell 6.5% in May to a seasonally adjusted annual pace of 1.001 million, the Commerce Department said Tuesday. That marked the first decline in four months and was bigger than the 3.7% drop forecast by economists.

The decline was broad-based across regions and type of construction. Single-family housing starts fell 5.9%, while multifamily fell 7.6%. April's surge in home building was revised down slightly to 12.7% growth from 13.2%.

"Housing has yet to establish clear upward momentum again," said Jim O'Sullivan, chief U.S. economist at High Frequency Economics.

Newly approved applications for building permits, an indicator of future construction, fell 6.4% in May to 991,000 on a sharp decline the volatile multifamily segment.

But in one bright spot, single-family permits jumped 3.7% to 619,000, their fastest rate of increase since September 2012. Single-family construction represents the bulk of the housing market and is considered a better gauge of demand.

In a note to clients, economists from IHS Global Insight described the rise in single-family permits as "one nugget of very good news."

Overall, Tuesday's report showed that the road to recovery for the housing industry remains a bumpy one. Home building came roaring back in 2012 and the early part of 2013, only to be derailed by a rise in mortgage rates and a run-up in home prices that sidelined many prospective buyers. Unusually cold weather further depressed housing-market activity over the winter months.

Some recent data point to strengthening activity. Housing starts grazed postrecession highs in April, while sales data for that month indicate buyers are coming back into the market. April sales of existing homes -- which account for 90% of the market -- rose for the first time this year, while sales of new homes jumped 6.4%.

The National Association of Home Builders' confidence index, released Monday, rose in June for the first time this year. But it reflected builders' continued concern with a lack of demand for new housing. The index rose to 49, just below the 50 level which indicates more builders generally see conditions as good in the market for new single-family homes than bad.

NAHB chief economist David Crowe said builders perceive potential home buyers to be "still hesitant" and that "builders are reacting accordingly, and are moving cautiously in adding inventory."

The nation's largest Realtors' trade group, meanwhile, says more new homes are needed to increase the supply of homes available for sale.

That would help to lower housing prices and put the industry on a healthier growth path. In a recent analysis, the National Association of Realtors noted that the rate of job creation -- a proxy for demand -- has far outstripped the pace of home building over the last three years.

Economists generally believe home building will pick up this year from the 930,000 units built last year and make a modest contribution to economic growth. Steady job growth and interest rates that are still low by historical standards should support demand while the pace of home building remains well below historical averages. Since the year 2000, 1.3 million new homes have been built each year on average in the U.S.

US
Many Displaced by Superstorm Sandy Still Wait for Housing Help; Rules, Including Some Tracing to Fraud Seen After Katrina, Slow Delivery of Billions in Federal Aid
By Laura Kusisto and Josh Dawsey
1304 words
16 June 2014
20:01
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

Seventeen months after Congress authorized up to $16 billion to fix homes wrecked by superstorm Sandy, tens of thousands of people still are living in damaged houses or paying rent on top of a mortgage as they wait for rebuilding help.

About 15,000 New York City residents are seeking aid, but city officials say only 352 have so far received a check or city-provided home construction.

In New Jersey, 2,032 homes are being built or repaired, for more than 11,500 homeowners deemed eligible for rebuilding help, according to the state department of community affairs.

The October 2012 storm damaged about 200,000 homes from New Jersey to eastern Long Island, N.Y., while killing 87 people in that region. Three months later, Congress approved the aid, to be distributed by New Jersey, New York state and New York City programs.

But delivering it has been slowed by a combination of federal rules invoked to prevent fraud and misspending after Hurricane Katrina, local rules, and certain missteps by local officials and contractors.

"I'm still homeless how many months later, and how many billions are sitting in the bank?" said Allison Galdorisi, wondering when work will begin on her battered Staten Island home.

The waits contrast with vows of quick help. The day after the storm, for instance, President Barack Obama said, "My message to the federal government: No bureaucracy, no red tape."

Complicating that goal have been requirements such as full-scale environmental reviews, taking two to six weeks, for any home rebuilt with federal money. In New York City, with thousands of homes to review, 50 assessors do the first stage of collecting data, and 30 analysts complete the second step of producing reports.

A mandate that low- and moderate-income people get priority helped prompt the city to create a complex, three-tiered system that left some applicants in limbo for months.

Applications were further slowed because the Federal Emergency Management Agency staff told homeowners to apply for government loans, which are subtracted from any other aid they are approved for. The city hired financial counselors who helped applicants demonstrate they couldn't afford to take on such a loan, and thus qualified for the full amount in grant form—another time-consuming process.

FEMA said it encourages applying for low-interest loans in certain situations because they are another source of disaster-recovery aid.

Applicants also have complained of lost paperwork and unresponsive staff involving some contractors hired by New York City and New Jersey.

Federal money is being distributed in three rounds, each of which requires a state or city government to submit detailed spending plans; these are subject to public review and approval by the U.S. Department of Housing and Urban Development. Only the first round, of about $5 billion, has gone out.

One reason for strict rules from HUD, the agency charged with long-term rebuilding after disasters, is that "they all remember very well the Katrina experience," said Jamie Rubin, director of New York state's Sandy recovery program.

After Katrina, the U.S. waived certain rules and let Louisiana send money to homeowners without requiring them to spend it rebuilding. Thousands took it and never returned. The Sandy relief program has avoided any significant fraud so far.

New York state has been relatively successful at distributing its chunk of federal aid, handing nearly $330 million to about 6,500 of 15,000 eligible homeowners, mostly on Long Island.

Along the New Jersey coast and in New York City, help has been harder to come by. Most boardwalks and amusement parks on the Jersey Shore are back, but some neighborhoods just off the beach are filled with boarded-up bungalows.

Marc Ferzan, New Jersey's top recovery official, said "onerous regulations" from Washington make it impossible to distribute money quickly.

The state has also been hobbled by its own missteps. In January, it cut ties with a firm managing two rebuilding programs after complaints from applicants and other performance issues. The firm, Hammerman & Gainer Inc., has said it met its contractual obligations. It and the state are negotiating a settlement.

New Jersey officials also have acknowledged using faulty data that led to improper rejections for some homeowners.

In New York City, where the waterfront has many blue-collar enclaves of homes passed down through generations, foreclosures are up in storm-hit neighborhoods.

Some residents are leaving by choice, selling homes cheaply or taking advantage of a state buyout program. In communities where most residents agreed to leave, such as Ocean Breeze and Oakwood Beach on Staten Island, the program has offered to purchase more than 500 properties.

Allison Galdorisi and Claire Watson are trying to hold on. Their bungalow in Staten Island's New Dorp Beach neighborhood was inundated. They need $173,000 to repair and elevate the home.

They applied for aid in June, but their case was held up until November by paperwork issues. In December, they learned they had been placed in the second tier of New York City's three-tier distribution system, behind people with lower incomes whose homes might have been less damaged.

They don't know when reconstruction will begin and are paying for both a mortgage and a rental home. "I'm going to be using all of my insurance money to pay rent and expenses," said Ms. Galdorisi, a 49-year-old real-estate appraiser.

The city's tier system was created in part because of a focus by former Mayor Michael Bloomberg on preparing for future storms with resiliency projects such as dunes and sea walls. Fearful that this priority would siphon funding from housing, city officials created the tiers to make sure people who most needed housing money got it if there wasn't enough for all.

The system also helped the city meet a HUD requirement that at least half of its aid go to low- or moderate-income people.

Some federal officials call the tier system overly complicated. "They spent months trying to pick winners and losers among homeowners," said Rep. Michael Grimm, a Staten Island Republican. "Meanwhile, they weren't giving a dollar to anyone."

Marc La Vorgna, a former press secretary to Mr. Bloomberg, said that among other things, officials were trying to satisfy Republicans who "complained about the risk of fraud and waste after seeing the disasters post-Katrina." Mayor Bill de Blasio's administration said it now is eliminating this priority system.

Mr. La Vorgna said right after the storm, workers made emergency heat, power and water repairs that let some 20,000 city families return home. "We directly injected $650 million into the damaged housing stock in 120 days," Mr. La Vorgna said. "The only difference between that lightning-fast program and this one: HUD."

In a statement, HUD said its "focus has been on helping families get back into their homes as quickly as possible, spending taxpayers' money wisely, and rebuilding the region to be more resilient." Secretary Shaun Donovan said this month HUD is working with New York City to find ways to cut red tape.

Mr. de Blasio has said at least 500 damaged city homes would be rebuilt and 500 reimbursement checks issued by September. Asked when he expects most homeowners who sought aid to have rebuilt their homes, he declined to say.

Heather Haddon contributed to this article.

Corrections & Amplifications Allison Galdorisi and Claire Watson live in Staten Island's New Dorp Beach neighborhood. An earlier version of this article incorrectly said that they live in Midland Beach in Staten Island. (June 18, 2014)

Ahead of the Tape
Ahead of the Tape
By Spencer Jakab
410 words
17 June 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

This Isn't Your Father's American Dream

To many Americans, middle-class success looks like a nice colonial with a lawn, white picket fence and two cars in the driveway.

Not only was that vision interrupted for millions during the housing crisis, but it may be outmoded. When investors parse economic data to see how close the U.S. economy is to getting "back to normal," they must redefine normal.

Take Tuesday's data on housing starts for May. April starts rose by 13.2% from March and jumped back above one million on an annualized, seasonally adjusted basis. Starts had only surpassed that one-million mark in November for the first time since the crisis.

Now, with delayed activity from a harsh winter less of a factor, starts are seen having cooled slightly to 1.03 million in May, according to a Wall Street Journal survey of economists.

One million starts is actually a modest level, even in a data series going back to 1959, and low on a population-adjusted basis. For example, rebased to the number of Americans in 1959, starts averaged 1.185 million through 2002 when the housing bubble began to inflate. The three-month average now stands at an adjusted 545,000.

History provides a cracked lens for anyone trying to gauge how far residential-construction activity will have to climb to revisit glory days. Even in the housing-bubble from 2003 through 2006, activity fell slightly short of the long-run, population-adjusted average. Yet there is little disagreement home builders got ahead of demand then. And today there is less demand than there once was.

In 1960, nearly three-quarters of adults were married, compared with barely half today, and people tied the knot six years younger on average, according to Census Bureau data. Furthermore, the fertility rate in 1960 was 80% higher, resulting in more babies born to a smaller population.

There is less need for self-standing suburban homes today and more for smaller, cheaper apartments. April's housing data reflect this, with multifamily starts up nearly 40% from a year earlier while single-family ones barely changed. No wonder June confidence figures from single-family home builders, reported Monday, remained in pessimistic territory for a fifth consecutive month.

The American dream just ain't what it used to be.

Jamaica Bets on Rezoning for a Delayed Boost; Median Home Prices Are Lowest in Queens, Drawing First-Time Buyers
Gabby Warshawer

13 June 2014
16:47
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

After decades marked by blight and lackluster economic growth, Jamaica, the large, south-central Queens neighborhood, was poised to benefit from new zoning rules in 2007, but then the bottom fell out of the real-estate market.

Community leaders say development is only now beginning to move forward with new construction. Examples of new work set to begin include an apartment building with 500 units as well as retail space, and a hotel with more than 200 rooms.

The neighborhood has long benefited from several economic tentpoles, such as courthouses, a college campus and more than a dozen landmark buildings, though they haven't added up to a cohesive whole.

It also benefits from Jamaica Station, a regional transportation hub that serves most Long Island Rail Road lines and has an AirTrain station to John F. Kennedy International Airport, which is just south of the neighborhood.

Jamaica's boundaries aren't universally agreed upon, but under commonly used borders, it stretches roughly from Hillside Avenue to the north, Linden Boulevard to the south, the Van Wyck Expressway to the west and 191st Street to the east.

Settled by the Dutch in the mid-17th century, Jamaica was a center of commerce in Queens until the first half of the 20th century, with banks, department stores and government buildings. The second half of the century was marked by white flight from the Irish-American enclave and the departure of major retailers such as Macy's. The majority of residents today are African-American, and there are large numbers of Hispanic and West-Indian immigrants.

The area's old zoning regulations had been in place since the early 1960s. The rezoning covers 368 blocks and allows for larger residential and commercial structures in Jamaica's downtown surrounding the transit hub.

"I wish we had made more progress since 2007, when the zoning allowed it, but it's exciting to envisage what can happen," said Carlisle Towery, president of the nonprofit Greater Jamaica Economic Development Corp.

The new apartment-and-retail structure is poised to be "the largest private investment in Jamaica in a long time," said Meredith Marshall, a managing partner with the firm planning the building, BRP Development Corp.

Mr. Marshall said proximity to the transit hub made the investment especially appealing.

The hotel will also rise in close proximity to the transit hub and have 220 rooms, replacing blighted properties, according to Mr. Towery.

Municipal structures include Queens Civil Court and the Queens Borough Public Library's Central Library. CUNY's York College also has a 50-acre campus. City landmarks include the Queens General Court House and Jamaica High School. In addition, there is the Loew's Valencia Theatre, one of five opulent Wonder Theaters opened by the Loew's chain in the New York City area in 1929-30 that is now owned by a church.

The neighborhood had one of the highest foreclosure rates in the city, and the effects of the subprime-lending bust are still being felt.

In 2009, at the peak of the foreclosure crisis, there were 2,136 notices of foreclosure in the neighborhood, according to statistics from the NYU Furman Center for Real Estate and Urban Policy. Last year there were 1,662 foreclosure notices in the area. In both 2012 and 2013 the area had the seventh-highest rate of foreclosure notice of all community districts in the city.

The median home sale price in the neighborhood between March and May of this year was $333,000, according to real-estate website Trulia.com. That was 30% lower than the median price in all of Queens during the same time.

The bulk of Jamaica's housing stock consists of one- and two-family homes constructed from the 1920s to the 1960s, with architectural styles such as capes and ranches predominating. It draws many first-time buyers and investors, according to brokers.

"Jamaica is one of the most affordable neighborhoods in Queens and it has improved quite a lot over the past 20 to 25 years," said Gregory Scotidas, a broker who has been active in the area for more than two decades.

Parks: The 11-acre Rufus King Park is home to King Manor, an 18th-century Dutch-style house that is a national and city landmark and is run as a museum. The park also has playgrounds and handball courts.

Schools: The neighborhood has a number of public schools, including P.S. 48, an elementary school with more than 400 students that received a B grade from the city in its 2012-13 progress report. I.S. 238, a middle school with an enrollment of around 1,600, received a C from the city in its last progress report.

Restaurants: Jamaica and Hillside avenues have a variety of ethnic eateries and restaurants, such as Papusa Market, a casual Salvadoran eatery. Margherita Pizza, on Jamaica Avenue, is known for its classic New York slices and pies.

Shopping: The Jamaica Colosseum Mall on 165th Street has more than 100 stores, many of them discount shops. Jamaica Avenue is the area's main commercial thoroughfare and has national retailers such as Old Navy and Walgreens.

Entertainment: Jamaica Multiplex Cinemas shows first-run movies.

A four-story building that sits on 25 acres of wooded land in Ossining has served over the decades as a home and training center for Maryknoll missionaries. Now the Roman Catholic religious society is hoping to sell the property to aid its work helping the poor.

"We felt if we weren't going to use the property, why not turn it into something more beneficial for the people we serve," said Mike Virgintino, a spokesman for the Maryknoll Fathers and Brothers.

Until last year, the 43,000-square-foot former convent housed lay missionaries, who are now in a renovated building on the main Maryknoll complex two blocks away. The idea was to incorporate the lay missionaries, who work closely with members of the Maryknoll religious orders, into the main campus.

For the 25-acre site, Mr. Virgintino said, the group is open to a variety of options: an outright sale; leasing the building and front section of the property and selling the rear portion; or leasing the property with an option to buy it in the future.

"The property on the market is surplus to us," he said.

As the value of real estate rises, more nonprofit organizations are likely to sell their land or buildings to pay for activities, said Naomi Levine, executive director of the George H. Heyman Jr. Center for Philanthropy and Fundraising at New York University. "It's hard to get support," she said. "If you have the property and it's worth a lot of money, now is the time to sell it."

Within the last four months in Manhattan alone, the nonprofit organizations the China Institute in America, the Federation of Protestant Welfare Agencies and the owners of the United Charities Building have put up for sale long-held buildings.

Unlike some nonprofit groups, the Maryknoll Fathers and Brothers aren't struggling financially, Mr. Virgintino said. The organization received generous donations leading up to its centennial celebration in 2011 and has steadily raised money over the past six years. Money earned from the property would help finance Maryknoll work with the poor, which has included a literacy program in Tanzania as well as the renovation and expansion of an eye clinic in South Sudan.

The first part of the building on the Maryknoll site was built in the 1920s, and the second in the 1950s, said Thomas LaPerch, a broker with the Houlihan Lawrence Commercial Real Estate Group who is marketing the property. The sprawling facility includes 36 dorm rooms, eight apartments, 17 offices, a large dining room, a library and a kitchen.

The Maryknoll Sisters purchased the property and built a convent on the foundation of a building that burned down in a 1924 fire, Mr. Virgintino said. The convent was their home when not doing mission work in foreign lands, he said. The Maryknoll Fathers and Brothers bought the property from the sisters in 1958.

Like other orders of Roman Catholic clergy, the Maryknoll Fathers and Brothers have experienced a declining membership starting in the 1960s, Mr. Virgintino said. In its heyday -- from 1920 to the mid-1950s -- the organization had 1,500 to 2,000 members at any given time. Today there are 363 Maryknoll priests and brothers.

The property is zoned for single-family homes on 1-acre lots, but the facility is also suitable for a range of uses -- such as a school -- with a special permit, Mr. LaPerch said. "There is nothing to build on in Westchester County," said Mr. LaPerch. "On the residential side, people are knocking down houses and putting up big houses. There is just no land."

So far, the property has drawn interest from other nonprofit organizations, including private schools, artist groups and a group that works on autism issues, Mr. LaPerch said. His focus is finding a tenant, but he said most interested organizations probably would prefer to buy because they will have to invest money in the buildings.

U.S. News: Wealth Hits High As Rich Get Richer
By Neil Shah
632 words
6 June 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Americans' wealth hit a fresh record in the first quarter amid a rise in home values and stock prices, a trajectory poised to continue as U.S. markets push higher but one that doesn't necessarily figure to rev up the sluggish recovery.

The net worth of U.S. households and nonprofit organizations -- the value of homes, stocks and other assets minus debts and other liabilities -- rose roughly 2%, or about $1.5 trillion, between January and March to $81.8 trillion, the highest on record, according to a report by the Federal Reserve released Thursday. The figures aren't adjusted for inflation or population growth.

Economists are hoping improvements in household balance sheets could help propel an economy that wobbled early this year when an unusually harsh winter took a bite out of growth. Recent reports on trade and the housing market have raised doubts about just how strongly the economy is likely to bounce back from the first quarter, when the economy contracted at an annual pace of 1%.

Michael Feroli, an economist at J.P. Morgan Chase, said rising stock markets and rebounding home prices aren't pumping up the economy the way they used to, largely because many of the gains are going to affluent Americans, who tend to save. Indeed, the so-called wealth effect -- the extent to which rising wealth leads to consumption and growth -- is roughly half as strong as that of previous economic expansions, he said.

Another gauge of Americans' wealth -- U.S. net worth as a share of disposable income -- remains slightly below levels seen in 2006, Fed data show.

The U.S. stock market rose in the first quarter, with the broad Standard & Poor's 500-stock index nudging up about 1% after a 30% gain last year. Real-estate values also rebounded strongly last quarter, helping spread the benefits of the nation's economic improvements. The value of stocks and mutual funds owned by households rose $361 billion last quarter, while the value of residential real estate grew more than twice that, $751 billion, according to the Fed.

The recent jump in the value of real-estate holdings "could increase the wealth effect," Mr. Feroli said, though he warned Americans' responses to housing gains generally "kick in slowly over time."

For many Americans, housing is the most important financial asset. As real-estate values rise, Americans are gaining more equity in their homes, which makes it easier to sell them, refinance debts, borrow and spend. A measure of owners' equity as a share of the value of real-estate holdings hit 53.6% in the first quarter, up from 51.6% in the fourth quarter, a postrecession high.

Another positive sign in Thursday's report: Americans are feeling a little more comfortable about borrowing.

Overall household borrowing rose at an annualized 2% pace in the first quarter, compared with 0.2% in the fourth quarter. While mortgage debt shrank 0.9%, the drop was less than the previous quarter's 1.1% fall. Other types of consumer credit, including student loans, grew 6.6%, above the fourth quarter's 5.3% pace.

After years of digging out from heavy debts assumed during the boom years, Americans are now finding their debt burdens easing. Total U.S. household debt was about 108% of disposable income in the first quarter, down from a peak burden of roughly 135% in 2007.

A more manageable debt burden, along with rising levels of equity in homes, could prompt American households to borrow and spend more at a time when unemployment remains high and wages are only starting to rise after years in the doldrums.

By Keiko Morris

2 June 2014
The Wall Street Journal

English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Even as New York City's commercial property market recovers, the purchase of a large delinquent commercial loan tied to a Midtown tower shows some creditors are still nursing wounds they suffered during the recession.

A $160 million commercial mortgage secured by a 22-story office tower at 119 W. 40th St. was sold to the Blackstone Group LLP earlier this year, more than four years after it fell into default.

In the end, creditors lost almost $49 million in the deal, according to Trepp LLC, a New York-based data firm.

At the same time, the loan's sale price -- $165.7 million -- is a sign that values of properties are rising to pre-crash levels, and the total value of delinquent commercial-property loans is falling.

Some of the larger delinquent commercial loans, including the one associated with 119 W. 40th St., have been in default for a while, said Joe McBride, research analyst for Trepp. "Seeing it get sold for as much as it did is a good sign," he added.

Many properties bought across New York City during the boom times came with high prices and a lot of debt, experts said.

The loan on the Stuyvesant Town and Peter Cooper Village apartment complex is among the city's largest commercial mortgages in default, with an original principal amount of $3 billion. The special servicer representing bondholders in that case recently took steps toward a sale later this year or in 2015.

The value associated with loans more than 30 days delinquent in New York City dropped in April to $3.7 billion, down almost 30% from $5.2 billion in 2011, according to Trepp.

Of the 10 metropolitan areas with the highest losses in the fourth quarter of 2013 for commercial mortgage-backed securities deals, New York had the lowest percentage loss at 25.3%, and Detroit had the highest at 60.5%, according to Moody's Investors Service Inc.

Like many commercial property investors buying before the financial crisis, the group that purchased 119 W. 40th St. in 2007 envisioned a renovated building that would command rising rents.

L.H. Charney Associates Inc., George Comfort & Sons Inc., and Fortis Property Group LLC formed a joint venture and bought the 1915 tower for about $162 million. L.H. Charney Associates and Fortis Property Group declined to comment; George Comfort & Sons didn't respond to inquiries.

The deal required another $42.7 million for reserves and closing costs, according to CWCapital Asset Management LLC, the special servicer representing bondholders. The group financed the deal with a $160 million mortgage and a $22.25 million mezzanine loan.

But renovations took longer than expected. Signing leases for market-rate rents fell behind schedule, according to a 2009 Fitch Ratings Inc. report on a pool of commercial mortgage-backed securities.

By the end of 2008, the office building had an average rent of $21.95 a square feet, far below the $45 a square feet appraisal for market-rate rent, according to Trepp.

The loan was transferred to CWCapital in June 2009 because the loan was on the verge of default, according to Trepp. The joint venture defaulted on the loan in July 2009, and CWCapital eventually started foreclosure proceedings in New York State Supreme Court in Manhattan.

Over the years, the joint venture filed motions alleging CWCapital had refused to release reserve funds set aside for repairs and renovations. In response, CWCapital said there were insufficient funds in the reserves.

Some tenants withheld rent because of renovation delays. Court documents for some of the tenant disputes show that the complaints against the property owner were dismissed.

Foreclosure of the property had been on schedule for mid-2014 when the debt was sold.

By 2013, the mezzanine loan, which had been acquired at a discount in 2007, was repaid at a negotiated lowered amount. CWCapital put the debt of the original mortgage along with other unpaid loans worth billions of dollars on the auction block.

Blackstone emerged as the highest bidder. It views the $165.7 million price tag as a "sizable discount" for an office building in the neighborhood that surrounds Bryant Park, said Frank Cohen, a partner in Blackstone's real-estate investment group.

Although the amount paid for the debt was about the same that the venture paid in 2007, creditors still lost money because of accrued interest payments, reimbursement of principal and interest advances made over the years and other fees.

"Bryant Park has become one of the most desirable office locations not only because of the attractiveness of the park but the proximity to Grand Central and Penn Station and quick access to Times Square," Mr. Cohen said.

Blackstone owns several buildings in the area, including 1095 Sixth Ave., which will soon house a Whole Foods Market on the ground and second floors.

The firm intends to improve the property at 119 W. 40th St. and eventually sell it for a profit.

Blackstone is in the process of negotiating an agreement with the involved parties to take ownership of the building without formal foreclosure proceedings, according to someone familiar with the discussions.

U.S. stocks rose on Thursday as investors weighed a weak report on economic growth and a strong one from the labor market.

The Dow Jones Industrial Average climbed 22 points, or 0.1%, to 16656 in midday trading. The S&P 500 advanced five points, or 0.3%, to 1915 and the Nasdaq Composite Index added 16 points, or 0.4%, to 4241.

Major indexes traded in a narrow band in below-average volume after the second estimate of first-quarter U.S. gross domestic product showed the economy contracted at a seasonally adjusted 1%, more than expected.

Investors largely brushed off the weak report, saying that it essentially told the market what is already known: that the economy was sluggish in the first three months of 2014, in large part because of harsh winter weather.

The latest GDP report does little to change the monetary-policy trajectory of the U.S. Federal Reserve, which is reducing its bond purchases but remains committed to keeping interest rates low for an extended period, according to Brian Jacobsen, chief portfolio strategist at Wells Fargo Funds Management, which manages about $242 billion.

"People are dismissing some of the weakness and expecting that there is going to be strength in the future," Mr. Jacobsen said. "It's not about what was, it's about what will happen. A lot of what will happen will be driven by central-bank policies."

Anastasia Amoroso, global market strategist at J.P. Morgan Funds, which oversees about $400 billion, was similarly unmoved by the weak economic reading, which she likened to "looking in the rearview mirror."

Rather, she is focused on more-recent economic data that has shown signs of improvement. Jobless claims in the week ended May 24 fell more than expected and remained near prerecession levels, according to the Labor Department. The number of Americans filing claims for new jobless benefits fell by 27,000 to a seasonally adjusted 300,000, versus an expected to decline to 319,000.

A separate report on pending home sales for April rose 0.4%, below expectations for a gain of 2%.

The yield on the 10-year Treasury note rose to 2.424% from 2.440% late Wednesday.

European markets were little changed. The Stoxx Europe 600 rose 0.1% to 344.51. Traders positioned for next week's European Central Bank monetary-policy meeting, at which the central bank is widely expected to announce new stimulus measures.

Crude-oil futures added 0.8% to $103.52 a barrel, while gold futures lost 0.3% to $1,255.60 a troy ounce.

The dollar fell against the euro and the yen.

In U.S. corporate news, Apple rose 0.8% after it confirmed late Wednesday that it would buy headphone and audio software company Beats Electronics and subscription streaming music service Beats Music for a combined $3 billion.

Even with the price cut, it is still the highest-priced property for sale in Utah, according to Mr. Benson.

He purchased the land—16 separate parcels—and built the home in the late 1980s. He and his wife have nine children and 56 grandchildren, he said, so he needed to ensure the house would be big enough to fit the whole family.

The result was what Mr. Huntsman called "one of the most grandiose homes ever built in America," with an indoor swimming pool, a fitness center and a 22-car garage. The furniture, much of which was custom-made for the home, is for sale along with the house. The home is decorated with the Huntsmans' extensive collection of Native American artifacts, but those aren't for sale, he said. (Mr. Huntsman disputed previous reports in The Journal that the house cost more than $100 million to build and furnish, saying he's not sure how much money he put into it over the years.)

The family used the property—which has a lake, a stream and hiking trails—in all seasons for years, he said. But he now owns a resort in Idaho, where he was born and raised, and spends much of his time there. "We don't use Deer Valley much," he said, noting that he is planning to keep his primary residence in Salt Lake City as well as his home in Hawaii.

Mr. Huntsman, 76, said he hopes the reduced price will help the home sell quickly, so he can use the proceeds to help fund construction of a new 220,000-square-foot research facility at the Huntsman Cancer Institute at the University of Utah in Salt Lake City.

U.S. Government Bonds Pull Back; Seven-Year Notes Drew the Lowest Foreign Investors' Demand in Six Months
Min Zeng
650 words
29 May 2014

The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

The roaring U.S. government bond market pulled back Thursday after the yield on the benchmark 10-year note hit an 11-month low.

The 10-year note's yield at one point Thursday tumbled to 2.401%, the lowest intraday level since June 21, 2013. At the end of the session, it rose to 2.445%, and the note's price was 2/32 lower, according to Tradeweb. When bond yields rise, their prices fall.

Traders said investors who deemed the price strength overdone cashed out some chips. As a sign buying interest was sated, a sale of $29 billion in seven-year notes drew the lowest foreign investors' demand in six months as the auction offered the lowest yield since October.

The yield, a key global benchmark to set long-term interest rates for consumers and businesses, has dropped nearly 0.2 percentage points in May. The yield traded at about 3% at the start of January.

Still, analysts and traders said factors that have boosted the allure of haven Treasury bonds remain in place: concerns over the uneven pace of global economic growth and firm commitment by major central banks to keep interest rates near zero for longer to support growth.

"Bonds are overbought so profit taking seems legit to me," said Adrian Miller, director of global markets strategy at GMP Securities. "But that's not to suggest yields have bottomed out."

Investors had scooped up bonds earlier Thursday after pending new home sales rose 0.4% in April, much lower than the 2% forecast. Federal Reserve Chairwoman Janet Yellen earlier this month has cited weakness in the housing market in recent months as a risk to the growth outlook.

Other data Thursday pointed to an uncertain growth outlook. The U.S. economy contracted in the first quarter for the first time in three years. On the positive side, initial claims for unemployment benefits dropped by a bigger-than-forecast 27,000 to a seasonally adjusted 300,000 in the week ended May 24.

"There is just relentless buying in Treasury bonds," said Charles Comiskey, head of Treasury trading at Bank of Nova Scotia. "Maybe the bond market is predicting that growth would be weaker than people think and the Fed may hold rates near zero longer than people think."

Mr. Comiskey said that buyers included Asian investors who see U.S. bond yields as more attractive compared with those of Japanese government bonds. The 10-year Japanese government bond yielded 0.566%.

"One thing is for sure-don't stand in front of the train right now," he said, referring to the rally in Treasury bonds.

Traders said month-end buying has been another boost for bonds. Some fund managers need to reshuffle their portfolios to align with benchmark indexes. On the last trading session of each month, new bonds replace maturing debt in bond indexes.

Erik Schiller, senior portfolio manager for global government bonds at Prudential Financial Inc.'s fixed-income unit, which oversees $405 billion, said he expects the 10-year yield could drop to 2.2% before the end of the year.

Falling yields this year have dealt a blow to bond bears this year who called for bond yields to extend last year's rise. Leading into the year, optimism reigned supreme that the U.S. economy would pick up more momentum from last year.

Traders said short bets--which wager that bond prices to fall and yields to rise--have been forced to dial back in recent weeks as the bond market's strength continued to confound bond bears.

"It is a tough time to fight against the rallying market,'' said Michael Franzese, senior vice president of fixed-income trading at ED&F Man Capital Markets in New York.

The S&P 500 hit a record, but the peak was reached against a backdrop of subdued trading activity.

"From a positioning standpoint, clients are under-invested," Mr. Crofton said. "There's a chase to increase exposure" to stocks.

The euro slumped to a fresh three-month low against the dollar as a measure of business sentiment in Germany, the economic engine of the euro zone, came in below expectations.

The weak reading heightened expectations that the European Central Bank will take measures to ease monetary policy at its next meeting in June, which could weigh on the euro.

Net wagers against the euro in the futures market increased to $1.6 billion in the week ended May 20, from $373 million in the previous period, according to data from the U.S. Commodity Futures Trading Commission.

World News: China: Exports, Factory Data Point to Stabilization
By Mark Magnier
656 words
23 May 2014
The Wall Street Journal Asia
AWSJ
4
English
Copyright © 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

BEIJING -- A pickup in China's exports and factory orders is likely to reduce pressure on the government for broad-based stimulus to boost an overall slowing Chinese economy, economists say.

A preliminary gauge of manufacturing activity released Thursday showed a bump-up in business for factories for May. That follows broader April export growth for China, as well as for South Korea and Taiwan, which supply components for many Chinese products shipped abroad.

Growing signs of life in China's export engine appear to result from consumers in the U.S. and Europe, which are China's major markets, prying open their wallets a bit wider.

While the improved factory sentiment is good news, even if sustained, it is likely only to cushion but not counteract a deceleration in economic growth that was already under way. And growth is being slowed further by a crumbling housing market, economists said.

"It seems that exports are bouncing back quicker than expected, but there are lots of signs of domestic weakness, especially in the property market," said Citibank economist Ding Shuang. Nonetheless, he said he expects no major fiscal or monetary stimulus in the near future. "This year we'll see weaker domestic demand and better export demand," he said.

The modest pickup in exports and factory orders follows the rollout of targeted government measures to prop up growth, chiefly accelerating spending on public housing and infrastructure, among others. But economists said Beijing can be expected to do more of the same, rather than reach for broad-based fiscal or monetary stimulus. "I don't believe monetary stimulus is necessary," said Fan Zhang, an economist at CIMB.

China's economy grew 7.4% in the first quarter from a year earlier, more slowly than the 7.7% pace in the final quarter of last year.

On Thursday, UBS lowered its 2014 full-year economic growth forecast for the country to 7.3% from 7.5% and its 2015 forecast to 6.8% from 7.0% because of worries over the property market. That lowered projection came a day after Moody's cut its outlook for Chinese real estate to negative from stable. New construction starts in the property sector fell 24.5% year-to-year in the January-April period measured by area, according to government statistics released last week.

Should Beijing want to strengthen its measures for growth, one of the biggest tools would be a cut in the amount of funds banks are required to keep in reserve. That would make more money available for lending but could also add to already high debt burdens, economists have said.

"We do not expect further macro easing," said RBS economist Louis Kuijs in a research note. "Policy makers will first want to see the impact of the easing already in the pipeline. Also, despite concerns in the markets about growth, from the perspective of policy makers, things do not look so bad."

Thursday's preliminary purchasing managers index released by HSBC and research firm Markit stood at 49.7 for May, a four-month high, compared with a final reading of 48.1 for April. A reading of 50 or above signals growth, while a number below that suggests contraction.

The survey's factory- and export-order components both rose above 50, which economists attributed to more orders tied to government spending on infrastructure and higher overseas demand. U.S. consumer spending rose 0.9% in March, the largest gain in more than four years. Retail sales in the battered euro zone also ticked up marginally, 0.3%, beating expectations.

The HSBC index tends to survey sentiment among smaller companies. Economists also expect an upturn in the official factory outlook to be released early next month, which tracks larger state-owned concerns.
