Cargill Unit Discloses Stake in Freddie Preferred; Such Shares Have Traded at Huge Discount Since Firm Was Seized by the U.S.
Nick Timiraos
324 words
10 April 2014
12:46
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

CarVal Investors, a distressed-asset firm that is a subsidiary of Cargill Inc., accumulated an 11.6% stake in a single class of preferred shares issued by Freddie Mac, according to a federal filing by Freddie on Thursday.

Hedge funds and other distressed-equity investors have scooped up the preferred shares of Freddie and its larger rival, Fannie Mae, over the past year on the bet that the companies will be profitable enough that even if the government follows through on its plans to liquidate the firms, enough money will be left over for preferred shareholders.

Fannie and Freddie have around 40 different classes of preferred shares outstanding that have a redemption value of a combined $33 billion, though they have traded at huge discounts to those values since the firms were seized by the U.S. and placed into conservatorship in 2008. Preferred shares are senior to the companies' common shares. They used to pay a dividend, but those payments ceased after the firms went into conservatorship.

Investors have bet on the preferred shares because those stand to be paid out ahead of the common shares if there are any profits left over after the companies are liquidated or restructured.

Currently, the government sweeps away all of the quarterly profits of Fannie and Freddie as dividend payments for their 2008 bailouts, but investors have sued the Treasury in federal courts to challenge those changes. The U.S. also owns $187.5 billion in senior preferred shares that would have to be paid off in any overhaul.

CarVal's stake is in the 5.1% series of Freddie preferred. It holds some 928,000 shares of that class. There are nearly 464 million shares of all Freddie preferred classes outstanding.

Ann Folkman, a spokeswoman for CarVal, declined to comment.

Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

MANSION --- Jumbo Jungle: When Shoppers Say No To the Jumbo --- Luxury rentals are looking attractive to some wealthy home buyers, amid higher prices and mortgage rates
By Lisa Selin Davis
871 words
4 April 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
M4
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Rising home prices and an increase in jumbo mortgage rates are leading some wealthy home shoppers into the arms of luxury landlords.

Steve Halpern, an agent at the New York-based real-estate brokerage Urban Compass, says about 50% of his clients are opting for rentals, many of them after losing bidding wars. They have decided to wait for the market to calm down. "It's not that they can't afford the loan," says Mr. Halpern. "They're not ready to take that risk for something as high priced as the market is bearing right now."

Mr. Halpern recently represented a couple, both real-estate attorneys, who sold their co-op on New York's Upper West Side and rented a three-bedroom, 3 1/2-bath condo in the same neighborhood for $13,400 a month. "For them, it's the time to sell, not the time to buy," he says. "A lot of wealthy people are still scared of taking on big loans and would prefer to wait out the market."

To be sure, luxury sales remain healthy overall. The number of homes sold for more than $1 million has been climbing, according to the real-estate website Zillow. These high-end sales increased almost 29% in 2013 compared with the prior year; the median price for those sales was $1.44 million, which was largely unchanged from 2012.

But some factors are starting to weigh on buyers, including mortgage rates. The average rate for the 30-year fixed-rate jumbo was 4.46% in March, compared with 4.02% in March 2013 and 3.9% in April of last year, according to mortgage information firm HSH.com.

This could prompt wealthy people to invest their money elsewhere, such as in the stock market or in small business, and not tie it up in housing.

"People who are looking at a $1 million-plus home are more likely to be making money in other ways besides real estate," says Gloria Shulman, founder of CenTek Capital Group in Beverly Hills, Calif., a mortgage bank and brokerage.

Ms. Shulman said some of her clients have been turning to the rental market while they prepare to buy a property and wait for a more favorable market for buyers. The complex mortgage-application process is another factor, particularly for the self-employed.

In New York, the current market conditions are a "discouraging trifecta for Manhattan buyers," says Alan Lightfeldt, a data scientist with real-estate website StreetEasy. Buyers face low inventory, higher interest rates and rising prices, making for a challenging spring in the housing market. Sellers, on the other hand, should expect that double-digit price increases will plateau.

Buyers looking for an advantage are turning to all-cash deals, both to be competitive in a seller's market and to avoid additional interest and mortgage fees, says Mr. Halpern. In the housing market overall, cash purchases without mortgage financing reached a 10-month high in February, according to theCampbell/Inside Mortgage Finance HousingPulse Tracking Survey. Over 30% of home sales that month were all-cash.

Still, there are reasons for buying a luxury home sooner rather than later. For one thing, rents are high. In 2013, there were 1,294 rentals on the market for over $15,000 a month in New York City, almost the same number as the year before, according to StreetEasy. But the median list price of rentals on the market for over $15,000 rose to $21,500 from $20,500, a 4.9% increase year-over-year.

Jed Kolko, chief economist at the real-estate website Trulia, says the slow rise of mortgage rates will likely continue, meaning it may be better to buy now in some areas. By Trulia's calculations, interest rates would need to reach 10.6% for renting to become cheaper than buying in most of the country. But Mr. Kolko says "there are some markets where renting might be cheaper than buying if rates get into the fives." These markets include Honolulu, San Jose, Calif., and San Francisco, where luxury listings are commonplace and home prices have been rising.

Closing costs, income tax brackets and length of occupancy all also affect the equation. The rent-versus-buy calculation, says Mr. Halpern, isn't just a matter of finances. "It's about where you are in life."

Here are some issues to consider:

-- How long will you stay: Whether renting is cheaper or more expensive than buying a home, says Mr. Kolko, "depends a lot on things like condo fees, or how long you plan to stay put." Hefty closing costs, for example, can be worth it if you plan to own the home for a long time.

-- Figure out your deductions: Your tax bracket, and how much you benefit from mortgage interest and property-tax deductions, affects the rent-versus-buy calculation. Those in higher tax brackets tend to benefit more from mortgage interest and property-tax deductions, says Mr. Kolko.

Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

• Figure out your deductions: Your tax bracket, and how much you benefit from mortgage interest and property-tax deductions, affects the rent-versus-buy calculation. Those in higher tax brackets tend to benefit more from mortgage interest and property-tax deductions, says Mr. Kolko.

Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

U.S. News: 'Wealth Effect' Drives Vacation-Home Sales
By Kris Hudson
728 words
3 April 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Sales of vacation homes are surging again, the result of rising wealth in higher-income households and renewed confidence in the housing market.

The number of second homes acquired for part-time personal use jumped 30% last year to 717,000 homes, according to an annual survey by the National Association of Realtors. The gain was the largest since the association started tracking second-home sales in 2003.

Although the number of second homes sold last year is well short of the high point of nearly 1.1 million in 2006, last year's jump signals a rapidly changing sentiment about the value of residential real estate, which just a few years ago was considered a poor investment amid the broad market bust.

Economists note that the vacation-home market was a beneficiary of last year's big gains in the stock market and rising home values, bringing buyers more wealth and confidence. The S&P 500 index of stocks posted a 30% increase last year, its largest since 1997. The median price of an existing home rose by 11.5%, the largest gain since 2005, according to the Realtors group.

That combination gives rise to the classic "wealth effect" in which people feel more wealthy and therefore more willing to spend. More households also sought to diversify by transferring gains from stocks and other investments into real estate.

Rising prices and favorable currency-exchange rates spurred Calgary residents Sherry and Phillip Hickey to buy a second home in Mesa, Ariz., last year to escape the Canadian winters. The couple, represented by Realtor Kris Anderson at Your Premier Team at RE/Max Excalibur, spent $357,500 on a three-bedroom, three-bathroom home where they intend to spend roughly five months a year.

"We just saw that things were looking better in the Phoenix market and prices were going up," Ms. Hickey said. "We wanted to get in before they went up too high and to make use of the Canadian dollar at par" with the U.S. dollar at the time.

Ms. Hickey, 56 years old, and her husband, 63, who own a small business in concrete forming, spent several decades of savings on the purchase.

"The housing market is in full-blown recovery mode," said Joseph LaVorgna, chief U.S. economist for Deutsche Bank Securities. "People are much more confident in the sector as a long-term investment [as shown by] their decision to purchase an extraordinarily discretionary item, a second home."

Overall, vacation homes accounted for 13% of U.S. home sales last year, up from 11% in 2012, according to the Realtor association survey. Their median price last year was $168,700, up 12.5% from 2012.

Regionally, the South accounted for most vacation home sales last year -- 41% -- likely due to its mild weather. Next was the West, with 28%; the Northeast, with 18%; and the Midwest, with 14%.

"The market definitely picked up for vacation homes in the Tampa Bay area overall last year," said Tony Baroni, who leads five real-estate agents at Keller Williams Realty in the Tampa Bay, Fla., area. "Our market had gone down so much [in price] that, even when it started recovering, buyers still felt like it was a great value."

The second-home surge also underscores a disparity in the market. More-affluent, move-up buyers trading into their second- or third-generation home have carried the U.S. real-estate market in recent years as would-be first-time buyers have been hobbled by strict mortgage-qualification standards.

Also, several measures show that household wealth reached new heights last year. Pay gains have favored better earners; U.S. Census Bureau data show that the top 60% of U.S. earners saw household income rise by 2.3% or more in 2012 while the bottom 40% saw gains of less than 1.8%.

Meanwhile, the Realtor association's survey, which covered 2,008 households, also found that second-home purchases by investors declined last year by 8.5% to an estimated 1.1 million. Such a drop has been widely forecast as home prices rise and the number of foreclosed and distressed properties declines. The survey doesn't include purchases by large, institutional investors.

Property: Record for Co-op Hits $70 Million, City Brokers Say
By Josh Barbanel
555 words
3 April 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
A22
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

The 16-room Fifth Avenue home of the late Edgar Bronfman Sr. has gone into contract for $70 million -- after a bidding war -- setting what could be a record for the sale of a Manhattan cooperative, brokers said.

The sale price was $5 million more than the $65 million asking price listed last month for the apartment of the former chairman of Seagram Co. and the former president of the World Jewish Congress.

The quick deal at a high price is an indicator of the strong demand for a limited supply of apartments in the upper end of the market, brokers said.

Several brokers familiar with the matter said that the listing brokers, Mary Rutherfurd, Alina Pedroso and Leslie Coleman, had negotiated a deal at the full asking price, when a second buyer stepped in and offered $5 million more.

The brokers, who are with Brown Harris Stevens, declined to comment on the transaction.

The co-op was listed by the family of Mr. Bronfman, who died in December at the age of 84.

The identity of the buyer wasn't immediately disclosed.

The apartment is a full-floor penthouse in a co-op at 960 Fifth Ave., on the corner of East 77th Street. The apartment has terraces on all sides, including one measuring 100 feet across that faces Central Park.

Besides a living room, formal dining room and library, the home has five bedrooms, 8 1/2 bathrooms, and four staff bedrooms, on a separate level, one floor below.

Brokers who toured the property said the Bronfmans had lived in the house for four decades, and that it needed considerable renovation.

Co-op sales are subject to approval by co-op boards, which sometimes reject buyers. If this sale goes through, it would far exceed the most expensive co-op sale.

The record is the $54 million purchase in 2012 of a penthouse at another Fifth Avenue building, 785 Fifth Ave., by David Geffen, the music mogul. Denise Rich, the socialite, was the seller.

In recent years, the prices of grand co-ops built before World War II have lagged behind those of new condominiums, many of which offer glass walls or huge windows.

The most expensive apartment sale to date is the $88 million sale of a penthouse at 15 Central Park West, by Sanford Weill, the former chairman of Citigroup Inc., to the family of a Russian billionaire.

Several apartments in new condominiums have been reported in contract at even higher prices.

But with a limited inventory of apartments on the market, co-op prices have been rising, too.

In the first quarter of 2014, the median price of a Manhattan co-op set a record at $760,000, topping records set in 2008 near the end of the last real estate boom.

One broker familiar with the property but not involved in the sale, said that the competing buyers sought the kind of old-fashioned elegant apartment in a fashionable building that is rarely on the market these days.

"This is a once in a lifetime kind of property," the broker said. "The terraces, the views, the way it is situated, properties like that just don't exist."

U.S. News: Apartment Rents Climb as Vacancies Drop --- Housing Prices Rise Amid Stiff Demand for Units and Lack of Supply, While the Cost of Buying Is Out of Reach for Many
By Conor Dougherty
643 words
2 April 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
A4
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Apartment landlords continued to push through hefty rent increases at the start of the year, although the pace of the rises was slightly weaker.

Rental rates increased 0.6% during the first quarter, according to data from 79 U.S. metro areas that was released Tuesday by Reis Inc., a real-estate research firm. While that was a little slower than the 0.8% rise in last year's fourth quarter, rents were up a hefty 3.2% from a year earlier and have risen 13% since rents began their upward trajectory in 2009.

And with vacancy levels falling, rents appear poised for further growth, according to Reis, which said the rental vacancy rate fell to 4% in the first quarter, down from 4.2% in the fourth quarter and half the level in 2009.

"Rents are at a higher base and still growing," said Ryan Severino, an economist at Reis. "They will likely keep growing for the next few years."

Rental pressure has been building for years, as rising demand has run into an undersupply of apartments. With employment rising slowly but steadily, more young people are forming households, usually by leaving their parents' residences or breaking off from groups of roommates.

At the same time, the cost of buying a home has increased dramatically during the past year as prices have rebounded and interest rates have risen from record lows. The median monthly home payment -- including principle and interest -- has risen 20% in the past year, said John Burns Real Estate Consulting. That has forced some entry-level buyers to continue renting.

Few markets are more challenging than California's Silicon Valley, where the twin forces of rising employment and a chronic housing shortage have sent rents skyward. Rents in the San Jose metropolitan area grew 1.1% in the fourth quarter to an average of about $1,724, the second fastest rise in the nation last quarter.

The burden of rising rents has fallen hard on young workers, who are more likely to be renting -- or, in many cases, looking to get their own place after years of underemployment.

Young people have seen their job growth pick up of late, but they still are below where they were before the recession. About three-quarters of 25-to-34-year-olds had jobs in February, down from a prerecession high of 80% in 2007.

Lyle Burlingame, 29 years old, is among those struggling to keep up. Seven months ago, Mr. Burlingame moved to Belmont, Calif., to take a job in the information-technology area for a health-care system.

At first, he rented a one-bedroom apartment for $2,000 a month, spending well over half his monthly income in rent. To lighten the burden, he subleased the place and moved into another apartment with a friend. He pays $1,200 in rent -- about half his take-home pay -- but is living in cramped quarters.

"I have the bedroom and he has the living room, which bleeds into the kitchen, and it's terrible," he said.

Mr. Burlingame is now on the hunt for his third residence in less than a year. He hopes to live in nearby San Francisco, where vacancy is 2.9% and the average rent is about $2,100 -- the highest outside of New York City.

Mr. Burlingame is hoping to find a house with roommates and is willing to pay as much as $1,400 a month. Meanwhile, he is chewing up much of his free time. "It's like having a second job that I need to do after work," he said.

China's New-Home Prices Moderate in March; Average Prices Decelerate for Third Straight Month
Esther Fung
692 words
1 April 2014
07:27
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

China's housing market showed a further slowdown in March as new data indicated that price growth moderated in more cities, with developers slashing prices to attract buyers.

Average new-home prices grew 10.04% in March from a year earlier, decelerating for the third straight month after February's 10.79% rise and January's 11.1% gain, data provider China Real Estate Index System said.

The property sector is a key engine of growth for the Chinese economy, with real-estate investment accounting for 15% of the economy. Policy makers have tried to balance the challenges of reining in frothy housing prices in large cities and sluggish demand and oversupply in smaller urban centers, particularly as they wrestle with slowing economic growth.

Many potential home buyers are staying on the sidelines as property developers cut prices, sales agents said.

Even in Beijing, some property companies have reportedly asked for lower-than-expected prices on new housing projects.

In mid-March, China Vanke, the nation's largest property developer by revenue, sold apartments in a project near the sixth ring road on the outskirts of Beijing at around 2,000 to 3,000 yuan lower than market expectations. Beijing authorities last year introduced a new type of affordable home that would be priced around 30% cheaper than comparable housing nearby, and pledged to supply a total of 70,000 such units by year's end.

Property price and sales data in March are typically more reliable indicators of the strength of market demand, as January and February are slower sales periods due to the Lunar New Year holiday.

Analysts said home prices would continue to cool in the coming months as many developers are eager to drive sales amid an oversupply of homes in some markets, especially in tier two and tier three cities. Home buyers are also facing a tougher credit environment compared with 2013.

"A lot of banks are now offering mortgage loans to first time home buyers at benchmark lending rates or at a 10% to 15% premium, and this relatively tight credit market has dented sentiment," said Frank Chen, executive director at CBRE Research, a real estate consulting company. Last year, buyers could get a 10% to 15% discount below the central bank's benchmark lending level.

Many property developers, emboldened by strong sales in 2013, bought more land to build up their land banks, but are now struggling with tighter credit and growing competition.

"Signs are mounting that the housing market in a number of cities is not just cooling but actually cracking," said Wei Yao, China economist at French bank Société Générale.

According to the CREIS survey, more cities showed a decline in housing prices, with 37 cities registering lower month-on-month prices compared with 36 in February. Prices in the 63 other cities covered in the survey were higher.

On a month-on-month basis, home prices in Shantou city in southern Guangdong province fell the most, losing 3.0%, and in Heze city in Shandong province, prices declined 2.9%. Attitudes toward home purchases as investments have also shifted, especially in smaller cities where the growth in value of homes has been slowing and evidence of overbuilding becomes more pronounced.

Home prices in many cities are starting to moderate amid slower economic growth and the introduction of other high-yielding investment alternatives such as online funds. Other wealthier Chinese people have also been active in buying homes abroad. Analysts say they expect slower housing sales this year compared with 2013's sharp upturn as many genuine home buyers made purchases last year.

But some analysts said that a property crash isn't in the cards for China in the near term, noting that housing demand still is strong in the biggest cities. "The government is targeting gross-domestic-product growth of 7.5% and M2 growth of 13%, and if these targets are reached, there would still be sufficient liquidity that would make its way into the property market," CBRE's Mr. Chen said.

Personal Finance
The Great Property (and Tax) Comeback; How the prices of luxury homes—but also estate taxes—are rapidly rising
Brett Arends
1145 words
28 March 2014
09:42
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

THE GOOD TIMES are rolling again in the country's toniest neighborhoods. Property prices in East Hampton, New York City and Beverly Hills were up about 10 percent last year, according to Zillow, a Seattle-based site that estimates home values. Near Miami, on the exclusive Fisher Island—which can be reached only by private ferry, and even then, only by those whose names appear on a special list—home prices are up by about a third.

Higher real-estate prices normally spell good news for sellers, but there's an associated cost that many tend to forget: A property will probably generate much higher estate taxes upon the owner's death than it would have a few years ago. What's more, estate taxes have been rising faster than property prices and asset values—and, some experts say, they are likely to rise even further. Yet some legal maneuvering may help cushion the blow.

Today, the federal estate tax kicks in on estates worth more than $5.34 million, with the rate topping out at 40 percent. Since anyone is allowed to leave an unlimited amount of money to their spouse, the effective exemption per couple comes out to about $10.7 million. The low point for the estate-tax rate came in 2010, when it was effectively zero—thanks to political dysfunction on Capitol Hill that led to a one-year hiatus on the tax. As a result, the heirs of a handful of billionaires—including late New York Yankees owner George Steinbrenner—got to keep hundreds of millions of dollars that otherwise would have gone to Uncle Sam.

Of course, those savings are not available today. Indeed, the growing economic inequality in America means that, if anything, estate-tax rates are likely to rise. Even as recently as the 1970s, the top federal estate-tax rate on the richest Americans was 77 percent. If the economic disparity continues to widen, today's 40 percent rate may seem temptingly low to a future Congress.

The good news? The federal tax code has more loopholes than a 100-foot scarf created by a team of injured knitters, including a couple of big ones that might be especially useful here. The first allows someone to transfer a home to an heir through a temporary trust known as a qualified personal residence trust, or QPRT (pronounced cue-pert). This trust can make time your friend, rather than your enemy, for estate-tax purposes, as it can sharply reduce the taxable value of a home. A $10 million home transferred to an heir via a seven-year QPRT, for instance, would only count as an $8.6 million home. Since the beneficiary wouldn't get their hands on the property for another seven years, the Internal Revenue Service would allow the estate to discount the value by a few percentage points for each year's delay.

Even better, once the home is transferred to an heir, all future price appreciation gets excluded from the taxable value. If, for instance, that private-island home increases in value by, say, 7 percent a year and you die 10 years from now, then without the trust your estate would have to pay taxes on a $20 million home. With the trust, the taxes would be on a home valued at $8.6 million. Depending on your exact circumstances, that difference could end up saving your estate as much as $5 million in taxes. There are some caveats, of course, the main one being that you have to outlive the trust to get the benefit. Otherwise, the full value of the home reverts to your estate. Moreover, once the home is transferred, it is in the hands of your heirs, even if you live for 20 more years.

Another little-known strategy is to transfer the home to a family-controlled limited liability company and then give shares in the LLC to beneficiaries each year. This could slash a future estate-tax bill a few different ways. First, a person could claim an estate-tax discount on the value of each share in the LLC, because it's a minority stake in a private company. So, even though a 1 percent share in a $10 million beach house in the Hamptons ought to be worth $100,000, for estate-tax purposes, it might be valued at only $85,000—or even less. Second, one could slice $14,000 more off the taxable value—for each beneficiary, each year—with the federal gift-tax exclusion. That could bring the taxable value of that $100,000 share down to $71,000. Finally, once a share is given, it and its future price appreciation are excluded from the estate, up to the $14,000 gift-tax exclusion per person. Put another way, even if a home is worth $20 million, its total taxable value to an estate would be considerably less once a person dies in 10 years' time—again, saving millions.

Some experts prefer the LLC route. Over time, it can allow for a "substantial wealth transfer at no cost to the parent," says Eric Rothenberg, a tax attorney with a law firm based in Needham, Mass. But the big issue with an LLC is determining how much of a discount can be claimed on each share. In the past, some have claimed a discount of as much as 40 percent, meaning they have transferred $100,000 worth of shares for as little as $60,000 in taxable value, "but that's aggressive," says Bob Glovsky, a financial planner at Colony Group, a wealth-management firm that is based in Boston and has around $3.3 billion in assets under management. He warns that claiming too much might mean a challenge from the IRS. If the government agency succeeds, the investor would lose the savings—and owe additional interest as well as penalties. For these reasons, Glovsky recommends opting for the QPRT instead of the LLC. What's more, the discounts used on a QPRT are based on tables that are published by the IRS—so they aren't open to challenge. And, Glovsky says, the trusts are often simpler to set up as well.

Most well-off folks, of course, remember to get sound legal advice on these kinds of matters. It might help during the process to keep in mind that some say the estate tax is among the least obnoxious forms of taxes, because it is levied neither on toil nor on entrepreneurship, but on the good fortune of inheritance. And even though you might ultimately have to pay the tax in the end, the U.S. tax code—fortunately—contains some breaks for the well-informed.

Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

MANSION --- Jumbo Jungle: Big Loans for Small Spaces --- Buyers in expensive markets like San Francisco and New York are turning to jumbo loans to help buy diminutive apartments with sizeable price tags
By Lisa Selin Davis
851 words
28 March 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
M4
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Even small spaces can require large mortgages in competitive markets like New York and San Francisco.

"It's not unusual to need a $1 million mortgage on a 700-square-foot property," says Peter Grabel, mortgage loan originator at Luxury Mortgage Corp., a mortgage firm based in Stamford, Conn. "We're setting all-time limits of cost per square foot."

Between 2011 and 2013, 95 condos, co-ops and single-family homes measuring under 800 square feet sold for more than $800,000 in San Francisco, according to the real estate data company Property Shark. One 360-square-foot condo sold for $950,000, or $2,639 per square foot.

In that same time period in New York City, 1,710 properties of less than 800 square feet sold for more than $800,000. The average price per square foot of these units was $1,423. The smallest unit, 410 square feet, sold for $855,000 in 2013, or $2,085 per square foot.

In New York, smaller units may be the better bargain: often the bigger the property is, the higher the price per square foot. Larger units in many neighborhoods are more desirable, thus more expensive and more likely to appreciate faster.

"New York housing prices have been trending higher with a bigger emphasis on larger, family-sized apartments, generally with three or more bedrooms and especially within the new development market," says Jonathan Miller, president and CEO of Miller Samuel, a real estate appraisal and consulting firm. "There have been sales of one-bedroom units priced from $2 million to $3 million that are neighbors with family-sized units selling well above $20 million." The reason for the high price of the small units is that they share the amenities and cachet of the larger units at the same address, says Mr. Miller.

But it isn't always easy to get a jumbo loan (defined as more than $417,000 in most markets and more than $625,500 in pricey areas such as New York and San Francisco) for a small space. Some lenders have minimum square footage requirements. According to Hillary Legrain, a mortgage loan officer with First Savings Mortgage Corporation, in Bethesda, Md., many jumbo lenders have a 500-square-foot-minimum size requirement on a condominium. "The reason behind that is that those size properties are less marketable," she says.

In markets like New York and San Francisco, a jumbo mortgage on a tiny place is less of an issue, since there are many comparable properties. According to the real estate website Zillow, high prices for homes under 800 square feet are concentrated in coastal markets. Along with San Francisco and New York, markets seeing luxury prices for diminutive homes include: Orange County, Calif.; Honolulu; Miami Beach, Fla; and the Nantucket-Martha's Vineyard, Mass., area.

On the other hand, in Greenwich, Conn., only one luxury property under 800 square feet was sold for more than $780,000 in 2013, according to Zillow. In markets like this, with only a smattering of these properties, securing a jumbo mortgage for a small home can be trickier.

"Lenders generally won't approve a mortgage on any size home unless they can get an appraisal that shows similar properties routinely sell the same amount or more as the property in question," says Guy Cecala, chief executive and publisher of Inside Mortgage Finance. "And getting those appraisals can be hard to get if you want to buy the 'best,' or most expensive, small home in a given area."

If there aren't many comparable properties, says Keith Gumbinger, vice president of mortgage-information firm HSH.com, "that might limit the number of lenders among whom the borrower would shop, and lenders who do make these loans may or may not have a full menu of product available." Mr. Gumbinger says that lenders may choose only to offer one product, like an adjustable-rate mortgage, or could potentially add a premium to either the interest rate or the fees.

But Mr. Grabel says the square footage likely won't affect the loan terms, provided the lender is comfortable otherwise. "As long as you can find an appraisal to show that the value is reasonable," he says, "there's no special treatment you'd get other than, 'Wow I can't believe it's so expensive.' "

Here are some issues to consider:

-- Expansion plans: If you purchase a small co-op or condo with an eye toward someday expanding into the unit next door, there's no guarantee that the co-op or condo board will approve the expansion.

-- Consider a larger down payment: In markets where there are few jumbo loans for tiny dwellings and the appraisal is an issue, buyers could consider a larger down payment to secure a government-backed or otherwise nonjumbo loan.

Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

"It's not unusual to need a $1 million mortgage on a 700-square-foot property," says Peter Grabel, mortgage loan originator at Luxury Mortgage Corp., a mortgage firm based in Stamford, Conn. "We're setting all-time limits of cost per square foot."

Between 2011 and 2013, 95 condos, co-ops and single-family homes measuring under 800 square feet sold for more than $800,000 in San Francisco, according to the real estate data company Property Shark. One 360-square-foot condo sold for $950,000, or $2,639 per square foot.

In that same time period in New York City, 1,710 properties of less than 800 square feet sold for more than $800,000. The average price per square foot of these units was $1,423. The smallest unit, 410 square feet, sold for $855,000 in 2013, or $2,085 per square foot.

In New York, smaller units may be the better bargain: often the bigger the property is, the higher the price per square foot. Larger units in many neighborhoods are more desirable, thus more expensive and more likely to appreciate faster.

"New York housing prices have been trending higher with a bigger emphasis on larger, family-sized apartments, generally with three or more bedrooms and especially within the new development market," says Jonathan Miller, president and CEO of Miller Samuel, a real estate appraisal and consulting firm. "There have been sales of one-bedroom units priced from $2 million to $3 million that are neighbors with family-sized units selling well above $20 million." The reason for the high price of the small units is that they share the amenities and cachet of the larger units at the same address, says Mr. Miller.

But it isn't always easy to get a jumbo loan (defined as more than $417,000 in most markets and more than $625,500 in pricey areas such as New York and San Francisco) for a small space. Some lenders have minimum square footage requirements. According to Hillary Legrain, a mortgage loan officer with First Savings Mortgage Corporation, in Bethesda, Md., many jumbo lenders have a 500-square-foot-minimum size requirement on a condominium. "The reason behind that is that those size properties are less marketable," she says.

In markets like New York and San Francisco, a jumbo mortgage on a tiny place is less of an issue, since there are many comparable properties. According to the real estate website Zillow, high prices for homes under 800 square feet are concentrated in coastal markets. Along with San Francisco and New York, markets seeing luxury prices for diminutive homes include: Orange County, Calif.; Honolulu; Miami Beach, Fla; and the Nantucket-Martha's Vineyard, Mass., area.

On the other hand, in Greenwich, Conn., only one luxury property under 800 square feet was sold for more than $780,000 in 2013, according to Zillow. In markets like this, with only a smattering of these properties, securing a jumbo mortgage for a small home can be trickier.

"Lenders generally won't approve a mortgage on any size home unless they can get an appraisal that shows similar properties routinely sell the same amount or more as the property in question," says Guy Cecala, chief executive and publisher of Inside Mortgage Finance. "And getting those appraisals can be hard to get if you want to buy the 'best,' or most expensive, small home in a given area."

If there aren't many comparable properties, says Keith Gumbinger, vice president of mortgage-information firm HSH.com, "that might limit the number of lenders among whom the borrower would shop, and lenders who do make these loans may or may not have a full menu of product available." Mr. Gumbinger says that lenders may choose only to offer one product, like an adjustable-rate mortgage, or could potentially add a premium to either the interest rate or the fees.

But Mr. Grabel says the square footage likely won't affect the loan terms, provided the lender is comfortable otherwise. "As long as you can find an appraisal to show that the value is reasonable," he says, "there's no special treatment you'd get other than, 'Wow I can't believe it's so expensive.' "

Here are some issues to consider:

• Expansion plans: If you purchase a small co-op or condo with an eye toward someday expanding into the unit next door, there's no guarantee that the co-op or condo board will approve the expansion.

• Consider a larger down payment: In markets where there are few jumbo loans for tiny dwellings and the appraisal is an issue, buyers could consider a larger down payment to secure a government-backed or otherwise nonjumbo loan.

Pending Home Sales Fall to Lowest Level Since October 2011; Housing Sector Faces Rising Interest Rates, Plunging Temperatures

WASHINGTON—The number of people signing contracts to buy previously owned homes fell again in February, a sign of harsh weather and rising mortgage rates restraining buyers ahead of the spring house-hunting season.

The National Association of Realtors said Thursday its seasonally adjusted index for pending sales of existing homes dropped 0.8% in February from a month earlier to 93.9, its eighth straight month of declines. Economists surveyed by Dow Jones Newswires expected a 0.2% rise in pending home sales for February.

February's reading was the lowest since October 2011, after hitting a recent peak of 110.8 in June. From a year ago, the pending-home-sales index was down 10.5%. An index of 100 is equal to the average level of activity during 2001.

"The report remains broadly disappointing as the spring rebound in housing activity will likely have to wait another month," said TD Securities strategist Gennadiy Goldberg.

The report measures signed contracts to purchase new homes. It typically takes one to two months to complete the sale.

Regionally, pending home sales fell in the northeast and south in February, but rose in the Midwest and West.

Thursday's report comes after a string of figures showed the housing market struggling. A stormy, cold winter may have led some potential buyers to stay indoors. Rising prices and mortgage rates could hurt affordability for some prospective home buyers.

Sales of newly constructed homes were down in February, the Commerce Department said earlier this week. Existing-home sales, which represent about 90% of home purchases, fell for the sixth time in seven months, the Realtors group said last week.

The average 30-year fixed-rate mortgage ranged between 4.23% and 4.37% in February, up nearly one percentage point from a year ago, according to Freddie Mac.

Home prices were up 13.5% in January from a year earlier across 10 major U.S. cities, according to the Standard & Poor's/Case-Shiller home-price report earlier this week.

The pending sales report comes as other elements of the economy show signs of thawing after a winter chill. Last month, the pace of hiring was the strongest since November. Manufacturing output and retail sales both advanced in February after declining in January.

Ahead of the Tape
Ahead of the Tape
By Justin Lahart
413 words
27 March 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Housing Recovery Needs More Than Thaw

Spring is here and so, too, is an important test of housing's strength. It isn't a sure thing that the housing market will pass it.

Housing-market data have been shaky lately, and there is little doubt a harsh winter played a role. Not as many buyers will tramp through snow drifts to an open house, and home builders aren't going to break ground on as many new homes when sites are frozen solid.

So few will be surprised, or fazed, if the National Association of Realtors report on pending-home sales Thursday is weak. Indeed, economists estimate the pending-sales index -- which measures contract signings for previously owned homes -- rose just 0.2% in February from January. That would put it 9.4% below its year-earlier mark.

As warmer weather arrives, home sales should heat up. But the thaw may be less than hoped for.

Although much of the recent weakness in housing has been concentrated in parts of the country most affected by winter weather, other areas haven't been spared. The January pending-home-sales report showed contract signings in the West were 17.5% below their year-earlier level. That trend looks to have continued last month: The California Association of Realtors reported Tuesday that its pending-sales index was 12.1% lower in February than a year ago.

One headwind housing faces is that homes have gotten less affordable. As of January, home prices were 12% higher than a year earlier nationally, according to CoreLogic, with prices up significantly more in some states. In California, prices were 20.3% higher. Long-term interest rates are higher, too, with the 30-year fixed mortgage rate at 4.32% in the latest week, according to Freddie Mac, versus 3.54% a year ago.

But more than prices and rates, the important factor holding housing back is a lack of first-time buyers. One sign of that: The Mortgage Bankers' Association's weekly index of mortgages extended for home purchases is 16.9% below its year-ago level and 61% below its decade-ago level. With unemployment high, wage growth meager and banks still cautious on extending credit, it is hard for younger Americans to find funds to buy a home.

Until that changes, the housing recovery's roots will be shallow.

House Builder Bellway Benefits From Increased London Focus; U.K. Firm's Average Home Price Rises to £212,071 in First Half
Ed Ballard
397 words
26 March 2014

The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

LONDON—Concentrating on London and the southeast helped U.K. house builder Bellway PLC to a 13.1% increase in the average price of its homes in the first half of the financial year, Chief Financial Officer Keith Adey said Wednesday.

Bellway said the average selling price rose to £212,071 ($350,570) in the six months to Jan. 31, from £187,426 a year earlier, as it posted a 73% increase in first-half pretax profit to £103.8 million.

Mr. Adey told The Wall Street Journal that the biggest contributor to the higher prices was Bellway's increased focus on London, where houses sell for 55% more on average than in the U.K. as a whole, according to data for January published by the Office for National Statistics.

London sales accounted for 25% of revenue in the first half, up from 18% a year earlier, Mr. Adey said. That was only partly the result of selling more houses. Bellway also sold higher-value properties—such as a development in Chelsea where 25 homes went for an average of £800,000 pounds, in comparison to the cheaper properties Bellway mostly sells in the capital.

House prices in the capital have also risen faster than the company expected. "Our experience is that where we're bringing new sites to the market that we maybe bought 12 to 18 months ago, we're seeing net pricing gains above expectations of 5% plus," Mr. Adey said.

Away from London, other factors helped push prices up, Mr. Adey said. For instance, in the north of England Bellway sold fewer starter homes and more large family houses.

Bellway is unlikely to increase its presence in London much further. "Our exposure in London could increase but I don't think it will increase vastly from the 25% of turnover we achieved in the first half," Mr. Adey said.

Although competition for land has increased in the southeast of England, Bellway isn't yet having trouble securing sites cheap enough to develop profitably, Mr. Adey said.

At 1015 GMT, shares were up 70 pence or 4.4% at 1,677 pence.

U.S. News: Winter Chills Housing Market
By Jonathan House
611 words
26 March 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

WASHINGTON -- New-home sales fell in February, the latest sign of severe weather and rising mortgage rates slowing the housing recovery.

Sales of newly built homes fell 3.3% from a month earlier to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 440,000, the Commerce Department said Tuesday. January's strong gain was revised down, to an annual rate of 455,000.

New-home-sales data represent a small portion of homes purchased in the U.S. and can be subject to large revisions. But they provide a more current gauge of market conditions than some other indicators because they are tallied at the moment a contract is signed rather than at its closing.

February's sales were below the 445,000 rate forecast by economists. The average rate for the first two months of the year is roughly unchanged from that for the final three months of last year.

"The trend in sales is flat," said Ian Shepherdson, chief economist at Pantheon Macroeconomics. "The upward trend of 2012 and early 2013 is gone."

New-home sales have been rebounding since 2011, but they have been rocky over the past 12 months as buyers grappled with rising mortgage rates and soaring housing prices. More recently, cold and stormy weather has discouraged some prospective buyers from going house-hunting.

Existing-home sales, which represent about 90% of home purchases, fell in February for the second consecutive month. These data represent sales contracts that were closed in February but signed a month or two earlier.

The Federal Reserve last week signaled a more aggressive path for future interest-rate increases than many investors were expecting, which could put more upward pressure on mortgage rates in the months ahead.

A 30-year fixed-rate mortgage averaged 4.32% for the week ended last Thursday, according to Freddie Mac. That is down slightly from the previous week but up nearly one percentage point from a year ago.

Recent home-price pressure, however, may be easing. The Commerce Department report showed the median price for a new home sold in February fell 1.2% from the same period a year earlier to $261,800.

A separate gauge released Tuesday showed strong house-price gains moderating slightly in January. The Standard & Poor's/Case-Shiller index of prices for 10 major U.S. cities rose 13.5% in the year ended in January, down from 13.6% in December. The 20-city price index advanced 13.2% year over year, down from 13.4% in December.

Regionally, "the Sunbelt showed the five highest monthly" gains, the Case-Shiller report said. Las Vegas led with a monthly increase of 1.1%, followed by Miami, up 0.7%. San Diego posted its best January gain since 2004, up 0.6%.

New York and Washington, D.C., each booked their highest year-over-year gains since 2006. Phoenix saw its annual rate decelerate the most, but it was still up 13.8% year over year.

Phoenix saw its annual rate decelerate the most, but it was still up 13.8% year over year.

Faltering home sales have sapped some of the momentum out of the rebound in new-home building, an important driver of economic growth. Housing starts have fallen for three consecutive months, and many economists are downgrading their estimates for housing investment this year.

A survey of economists released Monday by the National Association for Business Economics forecast a 9% increase in housing investment this year, down from a previous estimate of 14% and from a 12% gain in 2013.

U.K. Inflation Cools Further in February; Annual Rate at Lowest Level Since October 2009
Jason Douglas
428 words
25 March 2014
03:38
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

LONDON—Inflation cooled in the U.K. to its lowest annual rate for more than four years in February, strengthening the Bank of England's case for keeping interest rates low to underpin an economic recovery.

The Office for National Statistics said the annual rate of inflation in Britain fell to 1.7% in February from 1.9% a month earlier, driven largely by a fall in gasoline prices. Annual inflation was last lower in October 2009.

The slowdown in inflation in the U.K. mirrors an easing of inflationary pressures across the developed world. Faced with rapidly-cooling inflation in the 18 nations that use the euro, the European Central Bank has come under pressure to introduce new stimulus measures to head off the threat of deflation, or sustained falls in prices.

Analysts said the slowdown in inflation in the U.K. is unlikely to prompt similar calls in Britain, where inflation has outstripped wages for much of the past six years, squeezing consumers' spending power. But economists said the decline should cement BOE officials' commitment to keep borrowing costs down to nurture a stronger recovery. The BOE has held its benchmark rate at 0.5% since March 2009 and traders expect officials to keep it there until early next year to spur growth and reduce unemployment.

"At the moment, there is no need to consider tightening monetary policy," said David Kern, chief economist at the British Chambers of Commerce. "The current economic environment of low inflation and low interest rates should make it easier for businesses to plan ahead and invest."

Central bank forecasts published in February show officials anticipate that annual inflation will stay around the BOE's 2% target throughout the next two or three years.

ONS data suggest inflationary pressures in the U.K. economy remain subdued, supporting the BOE's forecast. Earnings rose just 1.4% on average in the three months to January, while figures Tuesday showed wholesale prices rose 0.5% on the year in February, the slowest annual gain since September 2009. Companies' raw material costs tumbled 5.7%, driven by a fall in the price of oil.

One area where prices are rising fast is in the housing market, data show, stoking fears the U.K. is at risk of experiencing a potentially damaging real estate bubble. Average house prices rose 6.8% on the year in January, the ONS said, compared with a 5.5% increase a month earlier.

Australia Ramps Up Warnings on Surging House Prices; Central Bank Points to 'Unrealistic Expectations' of Price Growth
James Glynn
501 words
25 March 2014
18:22
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

SYDNEY—Australia's central bank said it would continue to monitor a surge in speculative lending for property, saying some measures of real-estate risk appetite resemble boom-like conditions.

With roughly 40% of all mortgage lending now going to property investors in key states such as New South Wales, there is a danger that "unrealistic" expectations of home-price growth are building, the central bank said in its twice-a-year review of financial stability.

"The share of households favoring real estate has risen to a level approaching that of the early-2000s property boom," the reserve bank said in the report. At that time, house prices nationally roughly doubled in the space of five years, prompting the reserve bank to raise interest rates sharply.

The central bank has been ramping up its warnings in recent months over home prices, which are rising at close to 10% per year nationally. It first raised the alarm late last year when data began to reveal a disproportionate number of people buying homes for investment purposes compared with those seeking property to move into.

Interest rates have been at record lows since August to support a sluggish economy and guard against a rise in unemployment, which hit a decade high at the start of the year.

But the cheap cost of funds also has fed a surge in house prices across the country, the largest gains being posted in major markets such as Sydney and Melbourne where foreign investment—especially from China—is also helping to drive growth.

The surge has fed fears of a house-price bubble that would destabilize the country's banks and economy if it were to burst suddenly. Australian house prices have risen solidly over recent decades, avoiding the crash that happened in places such as the U.S. during the global financial crisis in 2008-09.

While near-term house price risks appear contained, the speculative property market will be "monitored closely for signs of excessive lending and lending practices," the central bank said.

The remarks come amid a debate about whether the country's prudential regulator should move to toughen rules governing lending for housing, to take some heat out of price growth.

The central bank said earlier this month that it had discussed so-called macro-prudential tools, looking at the experience of places such as New Zealand, where it is hoped that new lending restrictions will help tame house prices without damping the broader economic recovery.

The central bank on Wednesday also said that while there was a general undersupply of housing in Australia, there remained some risk that pockets of oversupply might occur, triggering price falls.

As a decade-long mining investment booms fades in Australia, economic growth is expected to remain weak through this year. The reserve bank said this month that interest rates were likely to remain low for "some time."

Global Finance: Rules Proposed on Home Appraisals
By Alan Zibel
374 words
25 March 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
C3
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

WASHINGTON -- U.S. bank regulators proposed rules aimed at improving the accuracy of home appraisals by boosting the qualifications of those assessing property values.

The move is part of a postfinancial-crisis attempt to overhaul the appraisal industry and ensure that those assessing a home's value have no hidden financial ties to lenders that could influence assessments. Appraisers have been criticized for too-high estimates of home values during the housing boom, as well as overly conservative estimates during the housing bust.

The 2010 Dodd-Frank law required bank regulators to establish minimum standards for state-led regulation of "appraisal management companies" hired by lenders to select appraisers. Such companies are often owned by banks or other real-estate firms and take a cut of the appraisal fee paid by consumers.

Lenders have increasingly turned to these companies in recent years to comply with regulations requiring that they use independent appraisers. The shift has prompted concerns from real-estate agents and home buyers that appraisals were coming in below actual value and were of poor quality, causing real-estate deals to fall apart. Lenders require appraisals to ensure that they aren't taking an unnecessary risk.

A 2010 research paper by economist Leonard Nakamura of the Federal Reserve Bank of Philadelphia found the appraisal process "can go awry and often has." Home appraisals have typically been inflated because lenders and sellers have an incentive to make a deal go through, he found. But when home prices fell during the housing bust, appraisers were likely to overshoot downward because of a high number of foreclosed homes flooding the market.

The proposal by the Office of the Comptroller of the Currency, Federal Reserve and other regulators mandates that appraisal-management companies hired by federally regulated banks use only state-licensed appraisers with "the requisite education, expertise, and experience necessary" to complete appraisals competently.

Major owners of appraisal-management companies include Wells Fargo & Co., DataQuick and Fidelity National Financial Inc. They didn't provide comments to email requests.

(See related letter: "Letters to the Editor: More Rules Aren't the Answer on Appraisals" -- WSJ April 8, 2014)

CHANGZHOU, China—Groups of angry homeowners put up banners and demanded their money back after Hong Kong-listed property developer Wharf Ltd. cut prices on new homes in an eastern Chinese city, in the latest sign of stress in the nation's property market.

Around 20 homeowners picketed outside a property showroom in Changzhou Saturday, demanding to meet executives of the developer. They said they wanted their money back after prices at the project, called Phoenix Lake Garden, were cut by as much as 16%, according to the protesters.

Meanwhile, there was also a small disturbance at a second project called Ambassador House in the same city after the same developer cut prices there. According to property agency Soufun Holdings, Wharf cut prices of 20 apartments in the project to 8,200 yuan ($1,317) per square meter, down from the average 11,000 yuan per square meter it recorded in recent months.

"Wharf, give us justice. Return us our hard earned money," read one of the banners, held up on bamboo poles outside the Phoenix Lake Garden showroom of a project for mid- to high-end apartments and villas.

"We aren't speculators. We just want an explanation from the developer," said one 35-year-old home buyer, who said he had bought an apartment and gave his surname as Wu. "This is very unfair."

Others said that as many as 100 people who had bought homes at the project had vented their frustrations outside the showroom over the past week.

Asked about the incidents, Wharf officials in Beijing didn't comment directly on the disturbances.

"China's housing market has already become very market-based, price adjustment according to market changes is a normal market behavior," Wharf said in a statement to The Wall Street Journal. The price adjustment at Phoenix Lake Garden is focused on selling off inventory, Wharf added.

After a four-year campaign by the government to cool spiraling property prices, rises in home prices are starting to slow and in some smaller cities they are weakening.

Growth in average housing prices in 70 Chinese cities moderated in February for the second-straight month though they were still nearly 9% higher compared with a year ago.

Mr. Wu said he found out about the price cuts from text messages on March 14 from the sales team announcing them and asking if he wanted to buy another apartment. "I've been here every day since March 15, and no representative from the developer has spoken to us yet," he said.

Guangzhou- based Agile Property cut prices of its luxury apartments in Agile-Star River project earlier this month. Agile said at that time it was a temporary sales promotion.

Property developers say privately there isn't enough transparency in land sales and land use, which sometimes give rise to overbuilding in many smaller cities.

Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

WASHINGTON—The Federal Reserve's annual test of big banks' financial health showed the largest U.S. firms are strong enough to withstand a severe economic downturn, a sign that many will get the green light soon to reward investors by raising dividends and buying back shares.

The Fed said 29 of the 30 largest institutions have enough capital to continue lending even when faced with a hypothetical jolt to the U.S. economy lasting into 2015, including a severe drop in housing prices and a spike in unemployment. The Fed's annual "stress tests" are designed to ensure large banks can withstand severe losses during times of market turmoil.

Only Zions Bancorp , a regional lender based in Salt Lake City, posted capital levels during the two-year-downturn scenario that didn't meet the Fed's minimum standards. The Fed said Zions had a Tier 1 common capital ratio of 3.6%, below the 5% level the Fed views as a minimum allowance. The ratio measures high-quality capital as a percentage of risk-weighted assets such as mortgages, commercial loans and securities.

The results could buttress the desire of banks—which have seen their profits soar amid an improving economy and severe cost cutting—to return more of that income to shareholders. The six biggest banks earned $76 billion in 2013, just $6 billion shy of their collective all-time high. All U.S. banks earned a record $154.6 billion, according to data compiled by SNL Financial. Some of the biggest financial institutions, including Bank of America Corp . and Morgan Stanley , haven't boosted dividend payouts since the financial crisis.

The stress-test results will be a factor in the Fed's decision next week to approve or deny individual banks' plans for returning billions of dollars to shareholders through dividends or share buybacks. But a good performance on Thursday's test is no guarantee, since the Fed also will consider more subjective factors such as the strength of a bank's internal risk management.

On Thursday afternoon, the Fed was expected to privately give banks a preliminary answer on their requests, setting up a week of jockeying for banks that were told "no." Firms will have until early next week to revise their plans or challenge the Fed's math before a final decision is issued Wednesday.

Paul Miller, an analyst at FBR Capital Markets, said the results bode well for banks' approval of capital plans. "There's nothing in here that I think is a big shocker," he said. Analysts this year are expecting distributions from banks to be the highest since at least 2007, according to estimates compiled by Thomson Reuters for The Wall Street Journal.

The first stress test took place in the immediate aftermath of the 2008 crisis and helped shore up confidence at a critical juncture for the American financial system. Regulators forced banks to cut their dividends in exchange for billions of dollars in government aid and lenders were prohibited from raising shareholder payouts without U.S. approval.

The Fed said the latest tests show banks are "collectively better positioned" to withstand losses than they were during the financial crisis, after which regulators pushed banks to hold more loss-absorbing capital.

Under the Fed's "severely adverse" scenario—a deep recession with a rising unemployment rate, steep drop in housing prices and a nearly 50% decline in stock prices over nine quarters—30 banks would have suffered total loan losses of $366 billion, trading losses of $98 billion and a net loss before taxes of $217 billion.

The Fed also raised the bar in this year's test by making its own projections for each bank's balance sheets, assuming that assets would increase during the stressful period rather than decrease as banks had assumed. That required the firms to hold more capital. This year's test also forced eight big firms to contemplate the default of their largest trading counterparty.

Several of the largest banks, including Morgan Stanley, J.P. Morgan Chase & Co ., and Bank of America , were ranked near the bottom of the pack in the Fed's side-by-side comparison of bank capital levels over a nine-quarter period of severely adverse economic conditions.

Charlotte, N.C.-based Bank of America was the worst performer among the biggest banks with a Tier 1 common ratio that dropped as low as 5.9% under the Fed's severe scenario, compared with 6.8% in last year's test.

Most firms fared better than they did under last year's test, although a handful, including Citigroup Inc ., Bank of America , and Fifth Third Bancorp ., had capital ratios dip lower than the 2013 results.

The second-worst Tier 1 common ratio among the big banks belonged to Morgan Stanley , at 6.1%. Capital ratios were as low as 6.3% at J.P. Morgan , 6.9% at Goldman Sachs Group Inc . and 7.2% at Citigroup . Those were far below firms such as Discover Financial Services , which fell only as low as 13.2%, and The Bank of New York Mellon Corp ., which fell only as low as 13.1%.

Several banks put out their own numbers after the Fed's release. Wells Fargo & Co . said its lowest capital ratio during the hypothetical downturn would have been 9.7%, higher than the Fed's 8.2%. Bank of America said its lowest ratio was 8.6%, compared with 5.9% from the Fed. J.P. Morgan put its lowest ratio at 6.5%, compared with the Fed's 6.3%.

At least one bank came up with results that were lower than the Fed's. BB&T Corp . said its Tier 1 common ratio was 6.8%, compared with the Fed's projection of 8.4%.

BB&T Chief Financial Officer Daryl Bible said the company was "happy with how our processes went."

Discover Financial Services was so confident about its performance that it disclosed on Thursday how much of a dividend increase—4 cents—it asked the Fed to approve.

Discover, Fifth Third and J.P. Morgan declined to comment.

RBC Capital Markets banking analyst Gerard Cassidy said as long as banks are over the 5% threshold, they should be allowed to increase dividends or buy back stock. "The bright line in the sand is 5%," he said. "If you're over 5%, you've passed. It's not a relative game."

Zions was subjected to the Fed's stress tests for the first time this year. The bank said previously it likely would resubmit its capital plan given its recent move to sell certain debt securities, a process it undertook as a result of the recently enacted Volcker rule. A Zions spokesman said Thursday the bank expects to resubmit its plan in April.

Corrections & Amplifications

This article and chart have been updated to reflect the corrected results for banks' minimum Tier 1 capital ratios that were issued by the Federal Reserve on March 21. The Fed corrected figures for 15 banks by 0.1 to 0.5 percentage points.

WASHINGTON -- A broad measure of home sales fell again in February, the latest sign of severe weather and worsening affordability undermining the housing recovery.

Sales of previously owned homes fell 0.4% in February from January to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 4.6 million, the National Association of Realtors said Thursday. That matched a forecast by economists surveyed by Dow Jones Newswires and was the sixth decline in sales in the past seven months.

"Existing-home sales remain in a rut," said BNP Paribas economist Laura Rosner.

Sales have been on the decline since hitting an annual rate of 5.38 million in July, a four-year high. Rising mortgage rates and soaring home prices have sidelined many prospective buyers, while cold and stormy weather has dissuaded others from going house-hunting in recent months.

Existing-home sales, which account for more than 90% of all home purchases in the U.S., fell in the Northeast and Midwest but rose in the South and West.

The National Association of Realtors said the median home price in February was $189,000, up 9.1% from a year earlier, in part because supply constraints are driving up prices.

The trade group said two million homes were available for sale last month, which represents a supply of about 5.2 months. It estimates a supply of 6 to 6 1/2 months represents a rough balance between buyers and sellers.

"Affordability is continuing to weaken," said Lawrence Yun, the trade group's chief economist, though he added sales activity should pick up after winter ends.

MANSION --- Spread Sheet: Million-Dollar Nabes --- Pricey listings flock together in cities like San Francisco and San Jose; the low square footage factor
By Sanette Tanaka
593 words
21 March 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
M4
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

In some parts of the country, $1 million buys a starter home.

Nationwide, 4.6% of homes are priced at $1 million and above, according to an analysis of home listings by real-estate website Trulia. But some areas are especially saturated with million-dollar listings.

In San Francisco, a whopping 43.5% of listings are priced at $1 million and above. Fairfield County, Conn., ranks second, with 29.7% of homes listed over $1 million, followed by San Jose, Calif., with 25.7% of listings topping the million-dollar mark.

"In terms of million-dollar listings, there has been a huge, huge influx," says Paul Ybarbo, an agent with Sotheby's International Realty in San Francisco. "If you're in a nice neighborhood and live in a nice building, it most likely will command a million-dollar price point."

According to Trulia, San Francisco homes listed for roughly $1 million have a median size of 1,774 square feet, but can be much smaller. Mr. Ybarbo has a $1.15 million listing, currently under contract, for a one-bedroom apartment in Nob Hill that measures just over 1,000 square feet.

Do the million-dollar listings translate to million-dollar sales? Typically, homes that sold for more than $1 million made up 1% of total U.S. home sales for the past decade. Since mid-2013, that number has risen to 2% of all sales, says Sam Khater, deputy chief economist at CoreLogic, which specializes in mortgage and real-estate information. He expects the percentage of million-dollar sales to continue increasing so long as the stock market continues to improve and interest rates remain low.

Million-dollar listings tend to be more common on the coasts and rarer in the Midwest and South. Less than 0.5% of listings are priced above $1 million in places like El Paso, Texas, and Dayton, Ohio, which are similar in terms of the number of overall listings.

Mr. Khater points out that two of the top three locations for high-price listings -- San Francisco and San Jose -- have thriving tech industries. "That says a lot about the changing nature of the economy," he says.

The spread between markets in the share of million-dollar listings is much larger than the spread in other home-price measures, like price per square foot, says Jed Kolko, chief economist and head of analytics at Trulia. "The prevalence of million-dollar listings shows how different the markets are at the extreme high end," he says. "Million-dollar listings are 50 times more common on Long Island [New York] than in Rochester, even though the median price per square foot is only 2.5 times higher."

Generally, metro areas with high concentrations of pricey listings tend to sport homes with smaller square footage, according to the data. In urban areas like Miami and New York City, the square footage is brought down even further by pricey condos.

The New York metro area comes in sixth place on Trulia's list, with 20.8% of listings priced over $1 million. These homes also boast the least amount of space at 1,489 median square feet. Bo Poulsen, a real-estate agent with Town Residential, recently sold a one-bedroom unit in West Village for $1.2 million. Its size? A mere 633 square feet, he says.
