Hedge Fund Magnate Makes Second Big

Hamptons Buy for $93.9 Million

Just months after his $75 million purchase of Wooldon Manor in Southampton -- the most expensive Hamptons deal in 2013 -- Scott A. Bommer is in contract to buy three parcels in East Hampton for a total of $93.9 million.

When the sale closes, it will be one of the priciest deals ever in the Hamptons.

Mr. Bommer, the founder of hedge fund SAB Capital, has signed an agreement to purchase adjoining lots on Lily Pond Lane that total 6.2 acres, according to people familiar with the situation. Two of the lots -- a nearly 2-acre oceanfront parcel with a large white house on it, and an adjacent vacant lot -- are owned by financier Maurice Cunniffe of investment firm Vista Capital. According to public records, Mr. Cunniffe bought the oceanfront lot in 1993 for $3.3 million, and the adjacent parcel in 1996 for $3.5 million. Mr. Cunniffe didn't respond to requests for comment.

The third parcel, measuring 2.8 acres, is owned by telecommunications executive Allen Salmasi, who purchased it in 2007 for $17.5 million, according to public documents. That property had been listed for $19.45 million and has a five-bedroom main house, a guesthouse and a pool, according to the listing. Mr. Salmasi couldn't be reached for comment.

Last year, Mr. Bommer and his wife, Donya, paid $75 million to buy the 14.5-acre Wooldon Manor parcel from fashion designer Vince Camuto. When the East Hampton properties became available, Mr. Bommer decided to purchase them, according to people familiar with the situation. So he's put Wooldon Manor back on the market for $98 million with Ed Petrie and Harald Grant of Sotheby's International Realty, and the Corcoran Group's Tim Davis.

Mr. Grant said a buyer could choose to purchase only part of the Wooldon Manor estate -- the house and surrounding 5.2 acres are asking $48 million, while the four adjacent lots are priced at $15 million each.

Mr. Bommer and his wife have also set real-estate records in New York City. Their 2008 sale of an apartment at 1060 Fifth Avenue for $48.8 million was a record price for a New York co-op at the time.

Oprah Winfrey Buys About 60 Acres in

Telluride for $10.85 Million

Oprah Winfrey has purchased about 60 acres of land near Telluride Ski Resort in Colorado, a spokesperson for Ms. Winfrey confirmed.

Ms. Winfrey plans to build a home on the site, according to people familiar with the sale.

In a deal that closed earlier this week, Ms. Winfrey paid $10.85 million through an entity called Yellow Brick Road CO LLC for the property in Mountain Village, according to public records. Bob Greene, her longtime friend and business consultant, helped facilitate the purchase.

The sellers were Carol Barnett, daughter of Publix Super Markets founder George Jenkins, and her husband, Hoyt Barnett. "We're happy that they're the buyers," Ms. Barnett said. "I know they'll do a great job with what they build." There are restrictions in place limiting the amount of building on the wooded parcel, Ms. Barnett said, but a main house and a guesthouse can be built there.

The property, off Rocky Road, can be accessed via Telluride's Galloping Goose ski trail and has "dramatic mountain views," said Sandy Sucharski of Telluride Real Estate, an exclusive affiliate of Christie's International Real Estate. Ms. Sucharski, who represented the sellers along with Telluride Real Estate's Steve Catsman, said the property wasn't on the market, but that the owners were approached about selling it.

Ms. Barnett said she and her husband have been coming to Telluride for 29 years, and own a condo in Mountain Village. They purchased this property about 20 years ago, with the intention of building a home there, she said. But as their children grew older, they spent less time in Telluride and the idea of building a house became less attractive -- especially now that there is gondola service in between Mountain Village and the town of Telluride.

"After that gondola was up and running and very convenient, it lessened our interest in building a house, since we already had a condo that was very convenient," she said.

Ms. Winfrey, chairman and CEO of OWN: Oprah Winfrey Network, is no stranger to Telluride. In fact, she first expressed interest in buying the Barnetts' property about 10 years ago, but they weren't ready to sell at the time, Ms. Barnett said. When Ms. Winfrey expressed interest again more recently, "we decided it was the right thing to do," Ms. Barnett said.

Matthew Hintermeister of Telluride Sotheby's International Realty, who isn't involved in the deal, said the parcel has views and ski-in and ski-out access, but is "really secluded." He added that Ms. Winfrey's new property is the largest in Mountain Village.

Modern Hamptons Home Sells for $20 Million

A Hamptons home designed by the late modernist architect Charles Gwathmey has sold for $20 million, according to the Corcoran Group, the brokerage that handled the sale.

Located in Sagaponack, N.Y., the nearly 8,000-square-foot, cedar-shingle home is built around an open-air courtyard. It has seven bedrooms, 11 1/2 bathrooms, a media room, fitness room and wine cellar. On the grounds there is a gunite pool with a hot tub, a tennis court and a pool house. The house, with a lead-coated copper roof, has a round turret intended to resemble a barn, in homage to the agricultural landscape. It also has deeded ocean access and a spiral staircase that leads to an upstairs office with an ocean view, said Corcoran's Susan Breitenbach, the agent who handled the sale.

Mr. Gwathmey, who died in 2009, designed a number of homes in the Hamptons throughout his career.

The property was sold to an undisclosed buyer by investor Jonathan Sobel, a former Goldman Sachs executive, and his wife, ophthalmologist Dr. Marcia Dunn. They purchased the 2 1/2-acre property for $2.575 million in 2003, according to public records. They then commissioned Mr. Gwathmey to build the home and furnishings for them, said Ms. Breitenbach.

The couple is moving because they are building a new home nearby, said Ms. Breitenbach, who declined to identify the buyers. The house was sold with all its furnishings.

The property was initially listed for $28 million several years ago, and its asking price was discounted several times as the listing changed agents. The home wasn't officially listed when it sold, said Ms. Breitenbach, because the buyers had decided to wait until their new home was ready before selling the old one.

Soap Included: A Fully Stocked L.A. Spec Home to List for $38 Million

A spec home in the Hollywood Hills of Los Angeles that comes fully stocked -- down to the towels, soap and even toothbrushes -- will soon list for $38 million.

According to owner Sean Sassounian, the roughly 13,000-square-foot, limestone-clad house has five bedrooms, seven full bathrooms and three half-bathrooms and comes fully furnished, with most pieces custom-made for the home. A massage room on the lower level comes with a table, towels and massage oil. Three bars throughout the house are stocked with high-end scotch, cognac and tequila, and the 1,100-bottle wine cellar is full. Some of the bathrooms even have toothbrushes in them, the owner said.

Mr. Sassounian, the 41-year-old founder of SAS Textiles who did this house as a side project, also decorated the home with art from his personal collection and said a local gallery chose other displayed pieces, including a work by Andy Warhol. The new house also has an elevator, three full kitchens -- including one outside -- a gym and a home theater. There is an eight-car garage and an infinity-edge swimming pool with city views.

Mr. Sassounian said he bought this property six years ago for roughly $4.5 million and demolished the existing home on the site. When asked why he went to such extremes to stock the new house, Mr. Sassounian said he wanted to use pieces consistent with the lifestyle of the eventual buyer. "Staging is very generic," he said. "I didn't think it would convey the right image." Instead, he "curated the best stuff I could get for the house."

Everything in the house is included in the sale price except the wine and the art, he said. While buyers can choose to buy the wine, the art is generally not for sale.

The listing agents are Alejandro Aldrete, Blair Chang and Mauricio Umansky of the Agency.

MANSION --- Landlords Of The Manor --- Some luxury homeowners are taking on tenants to fill empty rooms, help care for a favorite pet or just provide a little company; when a mansion becomes more like 'Melrose Place.'
By Stefanos Chen
1458 words
21 March 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
M1
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Corrections & Amplifications

A Princeton, N.J., guest apartment for rent has a Boffi-designed kitchen. A Mansion article on Friday about luxury homeowners renting out rooms incorrectly described the kitchen as having appliances by Boffi.

(WSJ March 22, 2014)

(END)

Lauren Duff, a 28-year-old accountant, lives in a Denver mansion she found on Craigslist.

"I took a gamble," says Ms. Duff, who signed the lease in 2009 with two roommates. She hit the jackpot: The listing was for a 1,900-square-foot, three-bedroom wing of an approximately 6,000-square-foot Mediterranean home that is on the National Register of Historic Places.

The owners, recent empty nesters, were looking for someone to check in on their French-style estate -- and their cat, Tigger -- while they traveled. "Lo and behold, these wonderful, young, professional tenants came along," says homeowner Bert Furmansky, a 68-year-old psychiatrist who lives under the same roof with his wife Dyana and three tenants. Dinner invites are common. The renters, who have their own entrance, parking spaces and pool access, pay a total of around $2,000 a month, a below-market rate for the area.

A surprising number of luxury homeowners are taking on tenants for reasons both pecuniary and personal. Whether looking for a pet-sitter or a like-minded neighbor, owners are seeking out these arrangements to make the most of their space. These set ups can work out well, though things can get messy when these homes go on the market.

It is a situation that may be more common than it seems. The Demand Institute, a nonprofit research group run by the Conference Board and Nielsen, surveyed more than 10,000 homeowners and renters nationwide. The study, released in February, found that 5% of respondents across all markets were "doubling up" -- when two or more unrelated adults live together. But that number was 7% in the most affluent housing markets, which it defined as roughly the top 10% of communities based on a number of socioeconomic measures, including median home value.

One possible reason is that luxury homeowners can be just as burdened by housing costs as the less affluent, says Louise Keely, chief research officer at the Demand Institute. Of the households in the top-tier markets that were polled, 41% said they spend 30% or more of their income on housing, which is the same percentage as in other markets.

These owners "have similar incentives to find ways to make their homes more affordable, and renting them out is one way to do that," Ms. Keely says. And despite home prices rising at double-digit pace over the past two years, she adds, rents relative to home prices are still high, which makes becoming a landlord all the more appealing.

In Washington, D.C., real-estate investors and property developers Jeff Printz, 43, and his partner, Chris Swanson, 45, were able to realize their unusual home project in part because of willing tenants. Their vision: to convert a 25,000-square-foot school into a luxury home with soaring ceilings, Carrara marble finishes and a master bathroom with a saltwater aquarium built into the shower wall.

The couple bought the late 19th-century building as a shell for $272,000 in 2000 and spent about $3 million to create a 9,400-square-foot duplex for themselves, plus nine additional rental units with personalized touches like replicas of the school's original chalkboards and a colorful mural of Albert Einstein. Now the rented units bring in about $21,000 a month, says their real-estate agent, Peter Lane of TTR Sotheby's International Realty.

"The rental market is hot all the time," Mr. Lane says about the D.C. area. There is a waitlist of tenants looking to move into the building, which includes a large pool area.

But close quarters has its downsides. "It is very 'Melrose Place' here sometimes," Mr. Printz jokes, referring to the soapy TV drama. Once, Mr. Printz was hosting a weekday cocktail party when he got a text from a tenant: "OMG it's Tuesday at 1 a.m. -- do you mind?" He was mortified, but contrite. "It takes some [nerve] to tell your landlord to shut up." Still, he says he's friendly with all the tenants, and invites them over regularly for parties.

Another downside: the potential consequences when the owner wants to sell. Looking to move onto their next project, converting a 1906 auto shop, Messrs. Printz and Swanson have listed their property for $6.5 million, down from $7.25 million in September. Mr. Lane, the agent, says part of the reason their home hasn't sold yet is the fact that the next owner will inherit nine tenants on one-year or month-to-month leases, all of whom expect professional rental services.

Gail Roberts, an agent with Coldwell Banker in Cambridge, Mass., says some buyers are averse to homes with existing tenants because of the legal hurdles that can arise, like a messy eviction. "Most people don't want to take on a house with someone else's leftover baggage," she says.

"It is very difficult to sell something with a tenant," adds Tom Daley, an associate broker on the board of directors with the Greater Capital Area Association of Realtors in Washington, D.C. He says having a legal rental unit in the home can boost the sales price and help the owner finance the property, but the trade-off can mean less privacy.

Ms. Duff, who scored the Denver mansion on Craigslist, may have to move on as well. Her landlord Dr. Furmansky has listed his home for $1.425 million with Ken Miller of Kentwood City Properties. So far most prospective buyers have wanted only the main house, but if one were interested in recombining the mansion by knocking down some walls, the tenants' wing could be purchased for an additional $460,000. Since the renters are on a month-to-month lease, the sale could force them to move. "It is going to break my heart if I stop living here," Ms. Duff says, in part because it has been so pleasant living with the owners. The couple have offered to sell the renters the unit, but it's unlikely they would buy it together.

Some homeowners say that having someone else in the home can be its own reward. "It is a big house, so it's kind of nice to have the company," says Ron Tanzman, a 45-year-old real-estate agent with Rodeo Realty who rents a part of his 6,500-square-foot Mediterranean home in Calabasas, Calif. He bought the house in 2012 for about $2 million, thinking he would share the home with his girlfriend at the time. When the relationship ended, he had a lot of home, and two lonely chow dogs to consider.

His housemate, Lori Amadei, a 37-year-old attorney, moved in about two months ago, after responding to a Craigslist ad. She pays $1,200 a month and a portion of utilities. Ms. Amadei checks in on Cashmere and Bleu, and is slowly introducing her cat, Tally, to the mix (she is a little shy, she says). Her only complaint is that the kitchen is much bigger than she is used to. "I hope I won't be spoiled," she jokes.

In Princeton, N.J., Rysia de Ravel, 67, wanted to create an attached, "mini me" version of her 9,000-square-foot, Prairie-style home for her mother. When she decided against moving her elderly mother into the space, she was left with a 1,200-square-foot, one-bedroom guest apartment with all the same high-end finishes as the main house. The apartment cost about $800,000 to build; the main house cost $6 million. A separate entrance opens to cathedral ceilings and clerestory windows in the living room, and leads to a gourmet kitchen with Boffi appliances and green marble countertops. There is also a garage. It is currently on the market for $4,500 a month with Callaway Henderson Sotheby's International Realty.

A former telecom entrepreneur, Ms. de Ravel says she is searching for an atypical renter. Her last tenant, a physician for a pharmaceutical company, stayed while on assignment nearby.

Previous homeowners in the neighborhood include presidents Woodrow Wilson and Grover Cleveland. "The people renting are like me," she says.

U.S. Housing Recovery Hits Hurdles; Rising Mortgage Rates, High Prices Constrain Buyers
Jonathan House

Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

WASHINGTON—A broad measure of home sales fell again in February, the latest sign of severe weather and worsening affordability undermining the housing recovery.

Sales of previously owned homes fell 0.4% in February from January to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 4.6 million, the National Association of Realtors said Thursday. That matched a forecast by economists surveyed by Dow Jones Newswires and was the sixth decline in sales in the past seven months.

"Existing-home sales remain in a rut," said BNP Paribas economist Laura Rosner.

Sales have been on the decline since hitting an annual rate of 5.38 million in July, a four-year high. Rising mortgage rates and soaring home prices have sidelined many prospective buyers, while cold and stormy weather has dissuaded others from going house-hunting in recent months.

Existing-home sales, which account for more than 90% of all home purchases in the U.S., fell in the Northeast and Midwest but rose in the South and West.

The National Association of Realtors said the median home price in February was $189,000, up 9.1% from a year earlier, in part because supply constraints are driving up prices.

The trade group said two million homes were available for sale last month, which represents a supply of about 5.2 months. It estimates a supply of 6 to 6½ months represents a rough balance between buyers and sellers.

"Affordability is continuing to weaken," said Lawrence Yun, the trade group's chief economist, though he added sales activity should pick up after winter ends. "Some transactions are simply being delayed, so there should be some improvement in the months ahead," Mr. Yun said.

New guidance from the Federal Reserve on Wednesday could put further upward pressure on mortgage rates in the coming weeks.

Though the Fed's official policy statement affirmed its plan to keep rates below their longer-run trend for the foreseeable future, the projections of individual officials did point to a slightly more aggressive path for interest-rate increases in 2015 and 2016.

The yield on the U.S. 10-year Treasury note, a reference point for many loans to U.S. businesses and consumers including mortgage loans, rose Wednesday in response.

A 30-year fixed-rate mortgage averaged 4.32% for the week ending Thursday, according to new figures from Freddie Mac. That is down slightly from the prior week but is up nearly one percentage point from a year ago.

Home-building activity, a key driver of broader economic growth, has also slowed in recent months. U.S. housing starts fell by 0.2% in February and by 11.2% in January, though building permits, an indicator of future construction, rose by 7.7% last month.

Other indicators have shown some signs the economy might be emerging from its recent soft patch. The pace of hiring last month was its strongest since November and manufacturing output recorded its biggest gain since August.

Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

Lauren Duff, a 28-year-old accountant, lives in a Denver mansion she found on Craigslist.

"I took a gamble," says Ms. Duff, who signed the lease in 2009 with two roommates. She hit the jackpot: The listing was for a 1,900-square-foot, three-bedroom wing of an approximately 6,000-square-foot Mediterranean home that is on the National Register of Historic Places.

The owners, recent empty nesters, were looking for someone to check in on their French-style estate—and their cat, Tigger—while they traveled. "Lo and behold, these wonderful, young, professional tenants came along," says homeowner Bert Furmansky, a 68-year-old psychiatrist who lives under the same roof with his wife Dyana and three tenants. Dinner invites are common. The renters, who have their own entrance, parking spaces and pool access, pay a total of around $2,000 a month, a below-market rate for the area.

A surprising number of luxury homeowners are taking on tenants for reasons both pecuniary and personal. Whether looking for a pet-sitter or a like-minded neighbor, owners are seeking out these arrangements to make the most of their space. These set ups can work out well, though things can get messy when these homes go on the market.

It is a situation that may be more common than it seems. The Demand Institute, a nonprofit research group run by the Conference Board and Nielsen, surveyed more than 10,000 homeowners and renters nationwide. The study, released in February, found that 5% of respondents across all markets were "doubling up"—when two or more unrelated adults live together. But that number was 7% in the most affluent housing markets, which it defined as roughly the top 10% of communities based on a number of socioeconomic measures, including median home value.

One possible reason is that luxury homeowners can be just as burdened by housing costs as the less affluent, says Louise Keely, chief research officer at the Demand Institute. Of the households in the top-tier markets that were polled, 41% said they spend 30% or more of their income on housing, which is the same percentage as in other markets.

These owners "have similar incentives to find ways to make their homes more affordable, and renting them out is one way to do that," Ms. Keely says. And despite home prices rising at double-digit pace over the past two years, she adds, rents relative to home prices are still high, which makes becoming a landlord all the more appealing.

In Washington, D.C., real-estate investors and property developers Jeff Printz, 43, and his partner, Chris Swanson, 45, were able to realize their unusual home project in part because of willing tenants. Their vision: to convert a 25,000-square-foot school into a luxury home with soaring ceilings, Carrara marble finishes and a master bathroom with a saltwater aquarium built into the shower wall.

The couple bought the late 19th-century building as a shell for $272,000 in 2000 and spent about $3 million to create a 9,400-square-foot duplex for themselves, plus nine additional rental units with personalized touches like replicas of the school's original chalkboards and a colorful mural of Albert Einstein. Now the rented units bring in about $21,000 a month, says their real-estate agent, Peter Lane of TTR Sotheby's International Realty.

"The rental market is hot all the time," Mr. Lane says about the D.C. area. There is a waitlist of tenants looking to move into the building, which includes a large pool area.

But close quarters has its downsides. "It is very 'Melrose Place' here sometimes," Mr. Printz jokes, referring to the soapy TV drama. Once, Mr. Printz was hosting a weekday cocktail party when he got a text from a tenant: "OMG it's Tuesday at 1 a.m.—do you mind?" He was mortified, but contrite. "It takes some [nerve] to tell your landlord to shut up." Still, he says he's friendly with all the tenants, and invites them over regularly for parties.

Another downside: the potential consequences when the owner wants to sell. Looking to move onto their next project, converting a 1906 auto shop, Messrs. Printz and Swanson have listed their property for $6.5 million, down from $7.25 million in September. Mr. Lane, the agent, says part of the reason their home hasn't sold yet is the fact that the next owner will inherit nine tenants on one-year or month-to-month leases, all of whom expect professional rental services.

Gail Roberts, an agent with Coldwell Banker in Cambridge, Mass., says some buyers are averse to homes with existing tenants because of the legal hurdles that can arise, like a messy eviction. "Most people don't want to take on a house with someone else's leftover baggage," she says.

"It is very difficult to sell something with a tenant," adds Tom Daley, an associate broker on the board of directors with the Greater Capital Area Association of Realtors in Washington, D.C. He says having a legal rental unit in the home can boost the sales price and help the owner finance the property, but the trade-off can mean less privacy.

Ms. Duff, who scored the Denver mansion on Craigslist, may have to move on as well. Her landlord Dr. Furmansky has listed his home for $1.425 million with Ken Miller of Kentwood City Properties. So far most prospective buyers have wanted only the main house, but if one were interested in recombining the mansion by knocking down some walls, the tenants' wing could be purchased for an additional $460,000. Since the renters are on a month-to-month lease, the sale could force them to move. "It is going to break my heart if I stop living here," Ms. Duff says, in part because it has been so pleasant living with the owners. The couple have offered to sell the renters the unit, but it's unlikely they would buy it together.

Some homeowners say that having someone else in the home can be its own reward. "It is a big house, so it's kind of nice to have the company," says Ron Tanzman, a 45-year-old real-estate agent with Rodeo Realty who rents a part of his 6,500-square-foot Mediterranean home in Calabasas, Calif. He bought the house in 2012 for about $2 million, thinking he would share the home with his girlfriend at the time. When the relationship ended, he had a lot of home, and two lonely chow dogs to consider.

His housemate, Lori Amadei, a 37-year-old attorney, moved in about two months ago, after responding to a Craigslist ad. She pays $1,200 a month and a portion of utilities. Ms. Amadei checks in on Cashmere and Bleu, and is slowly introducing her cat, Tally, to the mix (she is a little shy, she says). Her only complaint is that the kitchen is much bigger than she is used to. "I hope I won't be spoiled," she jokes.

In Princeton, N.J., Rysia de Ravel, 67, wanted to create an attached, "mini me" version of her 9,000-square-foot, Prairie-style home for her mother. When she decided against moving her elderly mother into the space, she was left with a 1,200-square-foot, one-bedroom guest apartment with all the same high-end finishes as the main house. The apartment cost about $800,000 to build; the main house cost $6 million. A separate entrance opens to cathedral ceilings and clerestory windows in the living room, and leads to a Boffi-designed gourmet kitchen with green marble countertops. There is also a garage. It is currently on the market for $4,500 a month with Callaway Henderson Sotheby's International Realty.

A former telecom entrepreneur, Ms. de Ravel says she is searching for an atypical renter. Her last tenant, a physician for a pharmaceutical company, stayed while on assignment nearby.

Previous homeowners in the neighborhood include presidents Woodrow Wilson and Grover Cleveland. "The people renting are like me," she says.

U.S. Housing Starts Fell Slightly in February
Eric Morath
398 words
19 March 2014
07:59
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

WASHINGTON—Unusually cold weather chilled new-home construction in February, though underlying data signals a possible spring rebound.

U.S. housing starts decreased 0.2% to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 907,000, the Commerce Department said Tuesday. The slight decline came after starts tumbled 11.2% in January, though that month's pace was revised up from the previous estimate.

Building permits, a sign of future construction typically influenced less by weather, advanced 7.7% to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 1.02 million last month. That was the best monthly gain since April.

The pace of starts and permits were both above economists' expectations.

The improvement in permits "is a welcomed reprieve" after January's broad fallout, said Lindsey Piegza, chief economist at Sterne Agee Group. But the overall report suggests "there is more than winter weather dampening construction activity."

Recent housing reports suggest the sector might not break out of a winter slump as quickly as other elements of the economy. Last month, the pace of hiring was the strongest since November and manufacturing output recorded the best gain since August.

Severely cold weather can prevent builders from breaking ground and discourage would-be buyers from shopping. But home construction faces other headwinds, including elevated interest rates, a shortage of skilled workers and lack of available land.

A 30-year fixed-rate mortgage averaged 4.37% last week, three-quarters of a percentage point above last year's mid-March level, according to Freddie Mac. Higher interest rates make buying a house less affordable for borrowers.

The pace of housing starts has doubled since the recent trough during the recession, but construction remains well below the 50-year historical average of 1.5 million starts a year.

The underlying data in Tuesday's report was split by housing type. The jump in permits was led by a 24.3% increase in multifamily properties, a segment that tends to be more volatile. But in a positive sign, single-family starts increased for the first time since November.

"The gap between starts and permits is widening," said Joel Naroff of Naroff Economic Advisors. "Since builders generally don't pay the money for permits unless they expect to do something with them, you can bet that once the warm weather returns, so will the bulldozers."

China Housing Market Continues to Slow in February; Average New Home Prices in 70 Chinese Cities Rose 8.19%

BEIJING—Gains in property prices in 70 Chinese cities continued to moderate in February after slowing in January for the first time in a year, as lending limits and concerns about more unsold homes hit demand.

Average new home prices in 70 Chinese cities rose 8.19% in February from a year earlier, according to The Wall Street Journal's calculations based on data released Tuesday by the National Bureau of Statistics. Home prices in those cities rose 8.98% in January. Before that, gains had accelerated every month for the past year, most recently with prices up 9.17% in December from 9.10% in November.

Month-on-month, prices rose 0.27% in February, down from 0.38% in January.

Stripping out public housing, home prices rose 8.64% year-over-year in February, against 9.50% in January. The average month-on-month increase in February was 0.28%, lower than January's 0.4% increase.

Home prices in China surged last year despite government controls on the property market.

Chinese officials didn't announce new policy measures for the property market at the annual session of parliament earlier this month, but Xu Shaoshi, head of the National Development and Reform Commission, the state planner, said the prosper sector has hidden risks. He didn't elaborate.

In third and fourth tier cities, many of which aren't covered in the survey, oversupply heightens the risks of a sharp correction and credit defaults.

In recent weeks, property developers in some cities such as Hangzhou, Changzhou and Ningbo have starting to offer discounts to clear inventory and to improve cash flow.

Some analysts say it is too early to gauge how much the property market might moderate in the coming months, as January and February are typically slower periods for home sales due to the Lunar New Year holiday. Sales in March and April are typically better indicators.

Average house prices in Guangzhou rose 15.7% in February from a year earlier, after jumping 18.6% in January. In Beijing, prices were up 12.2%, after rising 14.7% in January. Prices in Shanghai and Shenzhen gained 15.7% and 15.6% after rising 17.5% and 17.8% respectively in January.

Eric Morath

English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

WASHINGTON—U.S. home builders remained cautious about the housing market in March, suggesting a driver of the recovery might be sputtering for reasons beyond the severe winter weather.

Builder confidence in the market for single-family homes rose just one point from a month earlier to a seasonally adjusted 47 in March, the National Association of Home Builders said Monday. Readings below 50 indicate more builders view conditions as poor than good.

The increase, which was smaller than expected, comes after a drop of 10 points in February, the biggest one-month decline on record. Builders reported they were slowed by poor weather, but also said they were also hampered by difficulties finding labor and land.

"The soft readings in the past two months might be dismissed because of the challenging weather, but detail of the report suggests some fundamental softening," said Michael Moran, chief economist at Daiwa Capital Markets America.

Interest rates remain elevated from a year ago, making houses less affordable for borrowers. A 30-year fixed-rate mortgage averaged 4.37% last week, three-quarters of a percentage point above the mid-March level last year, according to Freddie Mac. Still, rates so far this year have remained below the recent 4.57% peak reached in September.

Monday's report showed sales and traffic increased slightly in March, but sales expectations over the next six months declined.

"A number of factors are raising builder concerns over meeting demand for the spring buying season," said NAHB Chief Economist David Crowe. "These include a shortage of buildable lots and skilled workers, rising materials prices and an extremely low inventory of new homes for sale."

Residential investment, which includes home building, had contributed to the nation's economic growth for 12 straight quarters until the final three months of 2013, when it became a drag on gross domestic product.

The survey showed the housing sector isn't bouncing back as strongly from the winter chill as other pieces of the economy. In February, U.S. employers added the most jobs since November. Retail sales increased last month after declining the previous two, and factory output rose sharply.

A warmer spring could aid home builders. Unseasonably cold weather can delay construction of houses and discourage would-be buyers from shopping. Regionally, builder confidence was highest in the West and weakest in the Northeast. The western U.S. has generally dodged the icy winter that hammered much of the rest of the country.

The industry faced substantial volatility last year, losing momentum in the summer when interest rates started to rise but regaining its footing in the fall.

The Federal Reserve this week will evaluate whether to again ratchet down its bond-buying program after cutting the pace of purchases twice since December to $65 billion a month. The stimulus program is intended to keep long-term interest rates low to spur stronger hiring and investment.

Central-bank officials will also update their forecasts for when the Fed will raise its benchmark interest rate from near zero. Changes to the federal funds rate influence rates on mortgages and other types of loans.

Markets
China Housing Prices to Face New Headwinds
Wei Gu
810 words
13 March 2014
04:23
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

There are two fundamental reasons to be bearish on housing in China: prices have risen too far too fast, and the government is tightening lending. But there are four additional, subtler reasons, and all are related to new reforms from Beijing.

China's home prices have doubled in the past five years, and they are still going up. In 2013, luxury property prices rose 17% in Beijing, the third-biggest gain among the top 30 global cities, after Jakarta and Dublin. In January, home prices rose in all but one of the top 70 Chinese cities, though the rate of appreciation has moderated.

Still, it is hard to see a property market crash in China. Underlying demand remains strong. The urban population continues to grow by 20 million people a year, and among existing urban residents at least 30 million households still live in units without a toilet or kitchen. Meanwhile, supply is stabilizing. Housing completions peaked at 10.7 million units in 2012 and declined slightly last year.

In his annual government work report this month, for the first time in almost a decade Chinese Premier Li Keqiang didn't issue a warning about curbing home prices. But investors shouldn't overlook changes in other areas, such as interest-rate and exchange-rate liberalization and land reforms, which are also going to hit the housing market.

"This government isn't interested in real-estate policies that merely scratch the surface," said Qian Shizheng, an accounting professor at Fudan University and a former executive at a state-owned property firm. "They are introducing policies that should correct the distortions and make properties less attractive as an investment asset."

Four under-appreciated factors are at play here. First, funding costs for banks have gone up, forcing them to pass on the higher costs to borrowers to protect their own profit margins, and to become choosier about borrowers.

Banks used to offer interest-rate discounts of up to 20% to attract mortgage demand. Now big banks are offering no discounts, while smaller banks are demanding a 10% premium on the regulated lending rates.

Higher interest rates are bad for asset prices. Rates should go up even more in the near future, as interest-rate deregulation accelerates. Chinese central bank Governor Zhou Xiaochuan said this week that banks should be able to set their own deposit rates within two years, adding that interest rates will initially rise as controls are removed.

Second, exchange-rate liberalization may encourage more money outflows. Demand for properties in China has been high because Chinese investors lack good investment options. But that is changing. Wealthy Chinese are already significant buyers of properties in the U.S., UK, Canada, and Australia. Once the capital account opens up, more middle class Chinese may start to diversify their assets abroad, reducing investment demand for properties at home.

Third, land reforms will have a mixed impact on demand. China is overhauling the process of converting rural land to urban use and strengthening farmers' property rights. On the one hand, these moves should increase rural incomes, making urban housing more affordable for farmers as they move to cities. This should be positive for housing prices, especially at the low end.

But more compensation for farmers also means less revenue for local governments, which rely on land auctions for half of their income. That will push more governments to enforce a property holding tax, which is already being done on a trial basis in Shanghai and Chongqing.

Last, anti-corruption and state-owned company reforms will also likely weigh on housing prices. China is building a nationwide database of home information. More transparency which will make officials who own multiple properties more inclined to sell, increasing supply in the short term.

The SOE reform may also have an impact. In 2010 the central government said that 78 SOEs whose core business isn't property should withdraw from the market. Although this may lead to reduced investment in new homes, less competition for land may lead to lower overall costs for developers, who can in turn pass the savings to buyers. This will also lead to cooling prices.

The government wants home prices merely to stabilize, which would please new buyers without antagonizing existing home owners. But that's a tough balance. "Given that each of the reforms is expected to progress at a measured pace, the individual impact on the property market from each one may be limited," said Oliver Barron, China research head for North Square Blue Oak, a UK broker.

"However, when the effects of capital-account liberalization, interest-rate reform and SOE reform are combined," added Mr. Barron. "We could see a notable decline in investment, both from companies that struggle to access capital and individuals who choose to invest elsewhere."

U.S. News: New-Home Building Is Shifting to Apartments
By Conor Dougherty
811 words
12 March 2014
The Wall Street Journal Europe
WSJE
6
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

The share of new homes being built as rental apartments is at the highest level in at least four decades, as an improving jobs picture spurs younger Americans to form their own households but tighter lending standards make it more difficult to buy.

Residential construction -- a pillar of the economy and employment -- is starting to ramp up again overall, but in previous years the growth was driven by single-family homes. Last year, according to census data, construction was started on a little less than one million new residential units, and about one in three of those was a rental in a multifamily building, the highest share since data began in the mid-1970s. Single-family homes accounted for about two-thirds of housing starts last year, down from their peak of 87% in 1993 and about 80% in the years leading up to the recession, the census data showed.

The move toward apartment construction reflects the convergence of several trends. Mortgage credit is still tight. Also, Americans have seen muted wage gains, and others have high student-debt loads, forcing people who otherwise would have bought homes to rent instead.

At the same time, as the job market improves, larger numbers of young adults are leaving their parents' homes and forming their own households -- adding more to the demand for rentals. And the supply of new apartments hasn't kept pace, especially in some key markets such as San Francisco.

"Builders are betting on more millennials leaving the nest this year," said Jed Kolko, chief economist at Trulia, a real-estate site. "But young people don't get a job one day and buy a home the next. The improving jobs picture for young adults will mean more renters this year, not a surge in first-time home buyers."

That shorter-term growth in rental demand is combining with long-run demographic trends that are expected to continue to tilt U.S. home construction toward "multifamily" units, a category that includes everything from garden-style apartments to towering condominiums. The baby-boom generation is moving into retirement and empty-nesthood, prompting many to downsize to smaller quarters. The generations behind them, meantime, are having fewer children, later in life, so need less space.

Jordan Rappaport, an economist at the Federal Reserve Bank of Kansas City, projects that by the end of this decade, single-family-home construction will top out somewhere around its pre-housing-boom level of roughly 1.3 million new homes a year. Multifamily units, by contrast, are projected to increase to an annual rate of 550,000 new units a year, or roughly twice the rate of today.

The single most important factor behind housing demand is household formation, which is economist-speak for people leaving one household to form another -- an adult child leaving mom and dad's place, for instance, or when a house full of roommates breaks up into smaller groups.

Take Devon Stansbury, 24, who graduated from the University of Cincinnati in 2012. Ms. Stansbury has moved four times over the past few years, living with her parents and roommates before settling in her own one-bedroom apartment in Dallas's Galleria district.

Like many in her generation, Ms. Stansbury believes she will continue renting into her 30s.

"I see [renting] for five years at the very minimum, maybe even up to 10," Ms. Stansbury said. "I am single, just focusing on my career. If something goes wrong here, maintenance will take care of it. And I feel safe."

Many economists expect the U.S. this year to finally eclipse the 3% annual growth rate that has eluded its economy since 2005. Whether that happens or not will depend to a great degree on how much new housing is built.

The category of gross domestic product that includes home building and renovations accounted for about 3% of growth last year, below the 4%-to-5% range that persisted before the housing boom and bust. And housing's overall contribution to the economy is actually several times as large when knock-on effects -- like commissions to real-estate agents or jobs at home-improvement stores -- are taken into account.

The housing sector already is starting to make up that ground, but a larger-than-normal share of it is coming from rental apartments. Champion Real Estate, a Los Angeles-based developer, has nine projects in the pipeline, totaling some 2,000 new units.

That is about three times as many as normal, said Bob Champion, the company's president. He expects the company to reach 15 employees this year, up from nine in 2012, and they are moving to new offices in June to accommodate them.

U.S. News --- THE OUTLOOK: Surging Housing Market Is a Two-Edged Sword
By Nick Timiraos
791 words
10 March 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

The U.S. housing market faces a challenge at the start of the spring sales season: higher prices.

It is hard to overstate the benefits of rising prices to the economy broadly and to homeowners, banks and home builders specifically after years of declines. Price gains have pulled more Americans from the brink of foreclosure and given home buyers more confidence that they won't get stuck with an asset whose value will decline.

But those gains have a painful edge, too, especially because prices have bounced back so strongly. The increases have rekindled concerns about affordability, particularly for first-time buyers, and could damp the gains of a housing rebound still in its early stages.

"Prices ran up so fast in 2013, it hurt first-timers' ability to become homeowners," said John Burns, chief executive of a home-building consulting firm in Irvine, Calif. "It's going to be a slower recovery than people had hoped because a number of people have been priced out of the market."

Home values nationwide are up 11% over the past two years, according to real-estate information service Zillow Inc., leaving values around 14% below their 2007 peak. Mortgage rates, which jumped a full percentage point to about 4.5% in the past year, have sharpened worries over housing affordability.

Even as prices have increased, housing still appears affordable by one traditional gauge. Since 1990, American homeowners have spent about 24% of monthly income on their mortgage payments, according to data from Morgan Stanley. Today, that payment-to-income ratio stands at around 20%, below the long-run average.

The problem with that view of affordability: It assumes borrowers have great credit and large down payments. The ratio isn't favorable for first-time buyers and others with lower incomes and smaller down payments, which increases their monthly financing costs. The payment ratio for first-time buyers was around 24% at the end of last year, in line with its long-run average, according to the Morgan Stanley analysis.

This pinch on first-timers is troubling because, so far, the housing recovery has depended to an unusual degree on cash buyers and investors. The relatively weak position of entry-level buyers could further suppress the homeownership rate -- now off more than four percentage points from its 2004 peak -- as more of them rent, said Vishwanath Tirupattur, a managing director at Morgan Stanley.

Rising prices are only part of the problem for first-time buyers. Inventory shortages and tougher mortgage-qualification standards benefit buyers who can make large down payments and those who can forgo a mortgage altogether.

Meanwhile, federal officials have repeatedly increased insurance premiums on loans backed by the Federal Housing Administration, which serves many first-time buyers because it requires down payments of just 3.5%. While mortgage rates at the end of 2013 reached their highest levels in more than two years, the all-in cost of an FHA-backed loan -- due to insurance-premium increases -- was closer to a five-year high.

Rising prices are less of a problem for current homeowners seeking to trade up because they can tap growing home equity to make their next home purchase.

Ideally, higher prices would stimulate more home construction, which would ease inventory crunches that are partly responsible for price increases while boosting job growth.

But builders have been slow to ramp up production, skittish after being caught with too much inventory when the 2008 downturn hit. Last year, many focused instead on higher-end houses, while entry-level construction was subdued. Sales of new homes last year rose by 14% from 2012, but the number of homes sold for less than $150,000 fell by 28%. Sales above $500,000 grew by 36%.

The worry is "a situation develops where construction remains low and prices continue to outpace incomes before first-time buyers can get in, and the next thing you know, you have to" bypass standard mortgage-qualification rules "to get people into homes," said Thomas Lawler, an independent housing economist in Leesburg, Va.

Others fret that low interest rates have allowed prices to rise too fast relative to incomes, which have stagnated. While homes are still affordable on a monthly payment basis because of cheap financing, homes no longer look like a bargain when comparing prices to incomes.

For the past few years, policy makers have focused on breaking a vicious downdraft in home prices. Now, it wouldn't hurt housing to see price gains flatten out, especially if income growth remains tepid. If not, the housing market's roller-coaster ride will continue.

English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

LONDON—A growing proportion of Britons expect interest rates to rise in the coming year, according to a Bank of England survey Friday.

The BOE said 40% of Britons polled in February expect the central bank's benchmark interest rate to rise from 0.5% in the next 12 months, up from 37% of those polled in November. The last time the proportion expecting a rise was higher was in May 2012, when 41% were anticipating a rate hike.

The survey found the proportion of Britons expecting rates to stay the same over the next 12 months fell in February to 37% from 43% in November.

The findings suggest British households' expectations of future changes in interest rates are moving closer to expectations in financial markets, where futures contracts suggest investors believe the BOE will begin tightening policy in the first half of 2015.

The survey found 50% of Britons expect the BOE's benchmark rate to be between 0% and 1% in 12 months' time. Officials have indicated future increases in the benchmark rate will be slow and limited.

Pollsters GfK NOP interviewed 3,949 Britons in early February to compile the survey.

The poll found Britons' expectations of future inflation declined in February. Respondents said they expect annual inflation of 2.8% over the next year, the lowest figure since May 2010, and 3.2% in five years' time. The BOE's target is 2%.

Respondents were also asked about house prices. Rapidly rising real-estate prices are stoking fears the U.K. may be facing a housing boom. Just under a third of Britons polled said they expect prices to rise between 0% and 5% in the coming year. In London and the southeast of England, where price gains have been quickest, 27% said they expect prices to rise between 5% and 10%.

MANSION --- Big Deals in Las Vegas --- Buyers emboldened by the recovering economy take advantage of reduced price tags in Sin City's struggling luxury market; an equestrian estate for $1.25 million
By Nancy Keates
1361 words
7 March 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
M1
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

In Las Vegas these days, the high-rollers may be the ones saving the most cash.

Chris Shelton, a real-estate investor representing an investment company, recently paid $2.8 million at auction for a 5-acre gated estate with seven bedrooms, a lagoon-style pool and a car museum in Tomiyasu Estates, about 10 minutes from the Strip. The estate last sold for $4 million in 2010. "The timing was right," says Mr. Shelton, who also snapped up another investment, a 17,000-square-foot equestrian estate on 11 acres in the Paradise Enterprise neighborhood for $1.25 million. The seller paid $3.75 million for that property a year ago.

At the high end of the Vegas housing market, homes are going fast. Sales of homes priced over $1 million almost doubled to 342 in 2013, compared with a year earlier, according to the Greater Las Vegas Association of Realtors. But while overall home prices in Las Vegas have risen over the past year, prices in the luxury slice of the market have struggled. The median price for homes over $1 million was virtually unchanged last year from the same level it has hovered at for the past five years -- around $1.4 million. The result: Buyers from pricier metro areas, like Los Angeles, are finding some steep discounts on luxury homes.

In November, Steve Aoki, a Grammy-nominated record producer and the founder of Dim Mak Records, bought a four-bedroom home in Summerlin, a gated golf-course community northwest of the city. At 15,600 square feet, the house is big enough for a music studio and a gym that has pits filled with giant foam cubes. The price: $2.8 million, $200,000 off the listing price. "The value was just insane," says Mr. Aoki, who is moving from a 3,000-square-foot home in Los Angeles.

The relative discounts at the high end are a contrast to the overall Las Vegas housing market, which has been bouncing back after a steep decline. Last year, Vegas home prices were up 35.5% over the previous year -- more than in any of the other 20 cities tracked by the Standard & Poor's/Case-Shiller price index. Much of the gain occurred because many foreclosures finally started selling. In 2013 some 62% of home sales were "traditional sales" -- not foreclosures or short sales -- compared with just 37% in 2012.

During the darkest days of the Las Vegas housing bust, most luxury homeowners sat on their homes, waiting for the market to improve. Now, real-estate agents say, they are returning to the market en masse, sensing a window of opportunity. And many are looking to sell quickly, having been spooked by the last downturn -- which means they are willing to negotiate on price.

"The higher-end homes have lagged in appreciation and people feel the timing may now be right to sell," says Dale Thornburgh of Synergy Sotheby's International Realty, who organized the auction where Mr. Shelton picked up his homes. At that same auction, a 3,905-square-foot, three-bedroom penthouse in The Palms Place Resort next to the Strip sold for $1.8 million to Texas banker Robert Marling. It was listed for $2.2 million. The seller was an investor named Lacy Harber, a Texas businessman.

Many of the biggest deals are in fairly new, upscale gated communities in the city's suburbs. These developments, which feature amenities such as golf courses, country clubs, parks and shops, were largely built during Las Vegas's superheated run-up in the mid-2000s. Some homeowners who bought in these developments -- which became emblems of the market's boom and subsequent bust -- are now eager to sell.

Cecilia and Lawrence Ventimiglia, luxury-home builders, bought their lot for $800,000 in 2006 and built an 8,000-square-foot, four-bedroom, 5 1/2-bath custom house on almost half an acre in the Ridges in Summerlin, a gated country-club development. When the market tanked, and similar lots in the same neighborhood were selling for half what they paid, they decided to stay in the house because they had too much money in it.

Even when they got a lot of lowball offers, they didn't sell. When the market started to improve last year, they decided to list it for $3.4 million -- and sold it for $3 million to Michael Mossholder, head of Global Marketing Partnerships at Ultimate Fighting Championship, a mixed-martial-arts promotion company. Though they said it meant a loss for them -- they won't say how much -- the couple said they decided to sell to Mr. Mossholder because they liked him and they were concerned that homes built more cheaply in their neighborhood during the downturn might erode the value of their home further if they waited.

Mr. Mossholder, who had been renting, had been looking for a new house for three years. "I wanted to be in this development, but people weren't selling" he says.

Many of the new luxury buyers in town hail from the same place: California. "Half my buyers last year came from California," says Zar Zanganeh, with LUXE Estates Collection. Last year 13.8% of all homes sold for $1 million or more in the Las Vegas area went to buyers from California. New York, in second place for out-of-state buyers, accounted for 1.4% of all $1-million-plus sales, according to San Diego-based DataQuick.

These buyers are attracted to Vegas's low prices -- and Nevada's low taxes. Many Californians have arrived in the wake of Proposition 30. Passed at the end of 2012, the measure hiked personal income and sales taxes.

Last spring, Joann and Vic Alfonso sold the home they'd owned in Palm Springs, Calif., for over two decades and moved to Las Vegas, purchasing an 8,500-square-foot, almost-new Mediterranean-style home in a guarded, gated country club community for $2 million. The "state of California is taxed to the limits and its economy isn't in good standing," says Ms. Alfonso.

The couple, who also later sold their home in Portland, Ore., "couldn't believe how much house" they were getting, adds Ms. Alfonso, who estimates a similar home in a similar neighborhood in Palm Springs would have cost three times as much.

For Ken Wolt, the move to Las Vegas was more about lifestyle than tax relief. The former head of a radiobroadcast group who acts in commercials and theater and does voice-overs, he was tired of the stress of Los Angeles (traffic, bad roads) and wanted a house big enough for a recording studio. He bought a partially finished, 6,500-square-foot house plus a guesthouse in 2010 for $1 million in a gated community and put about $200,000 into renovations. At first he was worried he'd miss the culture in Los Angeles, but he says he has found plenty of entertainment in Las Vegas.

Over the past five years, Las Vegas has started to more closely resemble Southern California. There are now more suburban gated communities with upscale shops. The once-grungy downtown is being revitalized. "Ten years ago people thought of Vegas as the Strip. Now a lot of people don't go to the Strip anymore," says Florence Shapiro, of real-estate firm Shapiro & Sher Group.

Even celebrities are trading up: Last May, musician Carlos Santana bought a house for $6 million in Summerlin. Last month, he sold his 7,200-square-foot contemporary down the street for $2.9 million. He had bought it in 2011 for $3.5 million. His new pad is 7,800 square feet and, according to the listing, has a $400,000 state-of-the-art movie theater, a game room, a gym, a putting green and an infinity pool.

China Vanke 2013 Net Profit Rises 21%; Housing Demand Was Stronger Than Expected in Major Chinese Cities, Vanke Says
Esther Fung
274 words
5 March 2014
22:17
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

HONG KONG—China Vanke Co., the nation's largest property developer by revenue, said Thursday 2013 net profit rose 21% as strong demand for homes buoyed earnings.

Net profit for the 12 months ended Dec. 31 rose to 15.12 billion yuan ($2.42 billion) from 12.55 billion yuan a year earlier, said Vanke, which is a bellwether for the residential market.

Revenue rose 32% on year to 127.45 billion yuan, the company said.

Shenzhen-based Vanke said in early January that contracted sales last year rose 21%, buoyed by stronger-than-expected housing demand in major Chinese cities. Contracted sales are an indication of what sales could be like months later.

In terms of floor space, Vanke's sales totaled 14.9 million square meters, up 15% from a year earlier.

Property sales in China grew faster than expected in 2013, as easier credit conditions and expectations that property prices would rise compelled buyers to rush into the market.

To contain runaway prices, Beijing has since early 2010 implemented curbs on second-home purchases and squeezed credit to developers amid concerns that unaffordable homes could threaten the economy and social stability. But the curbs were arbitrarily enforced and prices have continued to rise.

Overall sales in China are likely to moderate from 2013's jump, with the oversupply of homes in tier two and smaller cities dragging on price growth, analysts said. Property developers in cities such as Changzhou and Hangzhou have started to cut prices for new homes.

Superrich Pushed Prices of Top-Tier Homes Higher in 2013; Number of People With More Than $30 million in Net Assets Also Rose
Art Patnaude
602 words
5 March 2014
04:23
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

Estate agents, rejoice. The rich are getting richer and more confident, and there is nothing they like more than buying fancy homes.

New data from a global study of "ultra-high-net-worth individuals"—the top 0.01%—shows a stark rise in house prices in the most desirable parts of cities across the world.

The study, by real-estate broker Knight Frank, looked at the lifestyles of the superrich—those with more than $30 million in net assets—and the very expensive penthouses, castles, mansions and estates they buy.

Prices in prime locations rose last year in 54 of the 90 cities surveyed by Knight Frank. That's a greater proportion of gainers than in the prior year's survey, when half the cities showed rises.

The data suggest that the rich are spreading their wings, after years of retreating to a relatively small number of stable cities. When the banking crisis kicked off in 2008, a "big wave of money" flocked to investing in cities like London, New York, Sydney, and Vancouver, said Liam Bailey, global head of residential research at Knight Frank in London.

That wave is spreading out. Six of the 10 cities with the biggest average price gains in Knight Frank's Prime International Residential Index were in the Asian-Pacific region this year. Jakarta, Indonesia, was on top with a 38% gain.

Los Angeles was the only North American city in the top 15, posting a 14% rise.

While Europe continued to slump—the region lost 1% and contributed to 80% of all locations where prices declined last year —there were signs of recovery. Dublin captured the fifth spot on the index with a 17% gain, and Madrid posted an increase of 5%.

The ranks of the superrich appear to be growing. The study estimates that there were around 168,000 people who met the $30 million threshold last year, close to 5,000 more than in 2012. Their wealth totaled $20.1 trillion.

And they like property. Houses account for 30% of their combined wealth. On average, each of the superrich has 2.4 homes, the survey said.

Demand for mansions and penthouses across the world spread out from traditional havens like London and New York last year. While caution among the superrich—invoked by the half-decade-long financial crisis—continued to ease, their money continued to grow.

Alongside the uptick in wealth creation, confidence in the economic recovery lead investors to branch out from traditional havens, especially benefiting Asia. But not all markets in the region were strong. In Singapore and Hong Kong, governments moved to cool superhot markets.

Tighter lending regulation in Singapore and a stricter tax regime in Hong Kong "really slowed the markets down" in 2013, said Nicholas Holt, head of research, Asia-Pacific, at Knight Frank.

But the havens haven't lost their luster. Despite not showing huge price growth at the upper reaches, Monaco, Hong Kong, London, Singapore, Geneva and New York remain among the world's highest-priced luxury-home markets.

In those cities, homes in the most desirable districts are phenomenally expensive. In Monaco's top-tier residences, $1 million buys enough square footage for a bathroom. In London, it fetches a large dining room. For New York, it's a large reception room.

"You might think that if risk appetite has returned, it would undermine the safe haven story," Mr. Bailey said. "But no. Those prices are still rising."

Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

Vanke to Move B Shares to Hong Kong
By Esther Fung
562 words
4 March 2014
The Wall Street Journal Asia
AWSJ
22
English
Copyright © 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

SHANGHAI -- China Vanke Co. said it received long-awaited regulatory approval to convert its Shenzhen-listed B shares to Hong Kong-listed H shares, likely becoming the third Chinese company to successfully transfer its listing to the more-active Hong Kong market.

The property developer, China's largest in terms of revenue, said in a statement to the Shenzhen Stock Exchange Monday that it had received approval from the China Securities Regulatory Commission. It announced plans to convert its Hong Kong dollar-denominated B-shares to the Hong Kong exchange in January 2013, as it kicked off expansion plans offshore.

B shares are stock listed in China, generally denominated in foreign currency, and open to investment by non-Chinese. This is in contrast to A shares, which are denominated in yuan and are generally open to purchase only by mainland Chinese investors. H shares are the Hong Kong stock of mainland companies.

China Vanke's A shares will continue to be traded on the mainland.

The B-share conversion could enhance Vanke's name globally and help the company tap a bigger pool of investors for future capital-raising efforts, Vanke's board secretary, Tan Huajie, said when the conversion plan was unveiled.

"Will it excite the market? Yes, but it's not enough," Eva Lee, a property analyst at UBS, said of the approval. "Investors are now more concerned about macroeconomic fundamentals in the property market. China Vanke still had to wait a long time, which indicates that the Chinese authorities are still very cautious about giving companies another channel for liquidity," or capital raising.

China's property market has recently started to show signs of cooling, with developers in many cities starting to offer discounts on new homes amid concerns about oversupply and tightened credit. According to China Real Estate Index System, a data provider, average new-home prices rose 10.79% in February from a year earlier, compared with 11.1% in January. The rate of increase in December was 11.5%. The survey tracks new-home sales in 100 Chinese cities.

Analysts said it remains to be seen whether the approval results in a torrent of similar migrations among other Chinese firms.

China imposed a moratorium on new listings in 2012, and has only recently reopened its initial-public-offering market. China International Marine Containers was the first company to successfully convert its B shares into H shares in December 2012, followed by Livzon Pharmaceutical Group last month.

Bankers familiar with Vanke's B-share-to-H-share proposal said last year that the securities regulator had withheld approval on such conversions. Chinese companies have been looking to shift their listings from the illiquid B-share market, which was once a popular destination for foreign investors seeking to buy Chinese equities.

In addition to its B shares and yuan-denominated A shares, Vanke has a Hong Kong-listed unit called Vanke Property (Overseas).

It has invested in development projects in the U.S., Singapore and Hong Kong since the start of 2013. Last week, the Shenzhen-based developer said it made its second investment in the U.S. It will build a 61-story residential tower in New York City jointly with U.S. developers RFR Holdings and Hines.

Commercial Property: What's the Deal
965 words
3 March 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
A18
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Corrections & Amplifications

Angelo, Gordon & Co. and Deutsche Bank lent $80 million to HNA Property Holdings, a subsidiary of a Chinese airline, to refinance the Cassa Hotel. An item in the What's the Deal column on Monday incorrectly said that mortgage broker Massey Knakal, which arranged the transaction, had made the loan.

(WSJ March 5, 2014)

(END)

Chanel Sets Retail Record

With Madison Ave. Co-op

In a sign of the rising value of retail real estate in Manhattan, a modest-sized retail co-op on the Upper East Side has sold for $123.8 million, or more than $31,000 a square foot, one of the highest prices ever recorded for a shop property.

An affiliate of fashion house Chanel SA bought the 3,950-square-foot space, on the ground floor of a residential co-op building at 733-739 Madison Ave., at the corner of 64th Street; in the shop now is a Chanel Fine Jewelry boutique, said Aaron Shmulewitz, a partner at Belkin Burden Wenig & Goldman, which represents the building's co-op board.

The seller was Louis and Anne Abrons Foundation Inc., a philanthropy known chiefly for sponsoring arts programming at Henry Street Settlement on the Lower East Side.

"Everybody was shocked by this price," Mr. Shmulewitz said. "The foundation did very well."

As part of the deal, Chanel will pay $18,000 a month in maintenance fees to the board, and the board received more than $4 million in transaction fees from the sale, Mr. Shmulewitz said.

Chanel declined to comment; representatives for the foundation did not return calls for comment.

In the last few years, the highest per-square-foot price paid for a retail property was $15.409 in October 2012, when Richemont North America Inc. bought the space at the St. Regis Hotel on East 55th Street, according to Cushman & Wakefield, a commercial brokerage.

Asking rents on Madison Avenue from 57th to 72nd Streets have risen steadily over the last year, from $1,093 in the fourth quarter of 2012, to $1,356 at the end of 2013.

"Luxury retailers still want to be on Madison Avenue, because you know your client is going to be there," said Gene Spiegelman, a vice chairman with Cushman. "Retail real estate is one of the most in-demand categories for investors, especially prime, high-street retail."

-- Robbie Whelan

Massey Knakal Plays Role

Refinancing Midtown Hotel

Massey Knakal's new hotel sales division bagged its first big loan, helping a Chinese investor refinance its debt on property near Times Square.

The brokerage firm lent $80 million to HNA Property Holdings, a subsidiary of a Chinese airline, for the Cassa Hotel at 70 W. 45th St.

Beijing-based HNA Property Holdings acquired the hotel for $130 million two years ago, shortly after it opened but before all the rooms were complete and before the restaurant had opened. The hotel occupies the first 28 floors of the building; rooms for a Saturday night stay start at about 200.

The three-year loan was the largest that Massey Knakal's Capital Services division has made since it started in 2011, said Scott Aiese, a director of the division. The firm added a hotel specialist, Andrew Posil, six months ago.

"We are already working on new hotel loans," Mr. Aiese said.

-- Craig Karmin

Flushing Gets Posh Hotel

Pane Stone Construction Inc. has been based in Flushing, Queens, for the past 30 years and is now opening the neighborhood's first luxury boutique hotel.

The Parc Hotel is set to open March 10 at 39-16 College Point Blvd., which is just blocks from the Main Street transportation hub and not far from Flushing Town Hall and the Sky View Center.

"The majority of hotels in the neighborhood are all chains of various qualities, and we consider ourselves a higher-end establishment because of the detail and design," said Jimmy Hsu, who operates the building company with his parents and his brother.

Pane Stone Construction purchased the land for the hotel four years ago; construction on the 13-story, 96-room tower began more than two years ago, Mr. Hsu said.

The construction business and two silent investors have spent $12 million to 15 million on the hotel, including the land purchase, he said.

"We have a pretty good understanding of the Flushing neighborhood, it's very culture-driven," said Mr. Hsu.

Pane Stone Construction also owns several other venues in Flushing, including Minni's Shabu Shabu, a restaurant, and the Zebra Lounge and the Zebra Lounge 2.

The Parc Hotel will have five types of rooms, with the cheapest from $129 to $189 a night and the most expensive from $279 to $339 a night.

The hotel will have two restaurants, a rooftop bar and a lounge opening in spring, a dog spa, a gym, a business center and a meeting room; guests also can take a shuttle to and from La Guardia Airport and John F. Kennedy International Airport.

Lang Architecture opted for a modern design to make the hotel broadly appealing, said architect Drew Lang.

Flushing has a large Asian population and many of its hotels have a Chinese theme.

"There's a lot of glass in the rooms, windows floor to ceiling, so even the smallest rooms feel large," Mr. Lang said.

"What we paid most attention to is the facade, and we spent a lot of time sourcing grey limestone from Spain. We wanted to use a natural stone material to give the building a strong presence and allow it to weather over time -- we imagine it will be there for a long period of time, not just be fleeting."

-- Carmel Melouney
