Asia
Central Group Stays Upbeat Despite Thai Political Turmoil; CEO Says Nation Has Faced Political Strife Before and Business Is Still at 'Acceptable Levels'
Kathy Chu
1024 words
2 March 2014
06:49
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

Tos Chirathivat, 50 years old, became chief executive of Thailand's sprawling family-owned retail and real estate conglomerate Central Group in November as antigovernment protesters took to the streets, shaking the political and economic stability of the Southeast Asian nation.

The $7 billion retail empire, run by Thailand's second-wealthiest family, is the country's leading operator of department stores and supermarkets. Central Group gets about 90% of its revenue from its home market, but has grand ambitions for expansion in Asia and Europe.

Its Central Retail arm acquired Danish department store Illum last year and Italian department store La Rinascente in 2011. In 2013, Central Group's sales rose 19% to 234 billion baht ($7.18 billion), with its hotel and retail businesses expanding most rapidly. The group—whose Central Pattana PCL retail development arm and Central Plaza Hotel PCL division trade on Thailand's stock exchange—is targeting 2014 sales growth of 14%.

Mr. Tos, the grandson of company founder Tiang Chirathivat, recently spoke to The Wall Street Journal about Thailand's history of political strife, the difficulties of bringing his brand overseas and why the company doesn't need to be the world's number one retailer. Edited excerpts:

WSJ: How does it feel taking the helm of Central Group at such a turbulent time for Thailand?

Mr. Tos: Our country has been through this many, many times. This round is less violent, but it could last longer. What is important is that Thailand is growing up democratically. People come from all walks of life. They have expressed their views clearly. Future leaders will have to listen to the people.

I'm the head of the company, but we are a big family business. It's not like everything is on my shoulder. I have a lot of people to support me.

WSJ: How is the political unrest affecting business?

Mr. Tos: We have not closed any stores since November. Obviously, at the sites that are close to the protests, business is soft. The prolonged situation may affect the mood of the people and their spending. But business is still at acceptable levels.

WSJ: What is your vision for Central Group?

Mr. Tos: We are in the service industry, whether it's retail or real estate. Our goal is to be the leader in the service industry in Thailand.

The focus for us is on quality of assets, quality of offices. Our hotel group Centara is now number one in the country and expanding very quickly. Our aim is to be in each city and to develop all these services and become the center of the city.

WSJ: Do you expect to make more acquisitions?

Mr. Tos: Acquisitions are now a main part of our business. Everyone in the company has signed on to the idea. A company of our size really needs to grow, and if you grow organically, 10% or 12% would be pushing it. If you wish to grow at least 15% a year, acquisitions have to be part of the strategy.

WSJ: What is your war chest for acquisitions?

Mr. Tos: The overall investment plan for our group is 40 to 50 billion baht per year. It's hard to separate out how much will go to acquisitions.

WSJ: You've said that Europe and Southeast Asia are priorities for expansion. Can you be a strong player in both regions?

Mr. Tos: I don't think it's important for us to be the biggest in the world. Whether we are number one or number two, it's not important. We want to be profitable. We want to grow continuously.

The weakness of conglomerates is that they don't compete well with specialists. We have reorganized into eight business groups, so each group will grow more easily. By reorganizing, we want the groups to be more specialized. If you analyze our company, that's how we became successful in Thailand. Even if it's eight groups, they're all related and integrated.

Before, you might see the business groups setting up their own offices in different countries. Now, we sit down together and go into a country together.

WSJ: You've expressed interest in acquisitions in Indonesia. Any particular assets you have in mind?

Mr. Tos: Acquisitions are very sensitive, so it's hard to discuss. But in a country like Indonesia, with a lot of competition, it's impossible to start up an operation. It would have to be through acquisitions.

WSJ: What is the challenge for Central Group as it expands overseas?

Mr. Tos: So far, we have been in two places overseas. One is China, one is Europe. We are not doing well in China, and in Europe, we are doing well. Starting an operation in a foreign country is very difficult. The key is to make the right acquisition. You also need to have very good country managers and full support from the head office, otherwise it will be very difficult.

WSJ: Why is China challenging?

Mr. Tos: In the department store industry, no one is doing well in China. There are too many players, too many stores. China's slowdown also has an effect. The government crackdown on official spending and gifts, that directly hurts retailers. Consumers are also more cautious with spending. We've been in China for five years, and we're not yet turning a profit. Hopefully, that will [change] in three years.

Education: Bachelor's Degree in Economics, Wesleyan University, 1985; Master's Degree in Business Administration, Columbia University, 1987.

Career:Before joining the family business, Mr. Tos worked as an investment banker in Bangkok for Citibank. In 1989, he joined Central Pattana, the shopping mall development arm of Central Group. In his more than two decades with the family business, he has served as chief executive officer of Big C Supercenter, a hypermarket chain, and in various roles at Central Retail Corp.

Family: Wife Sukta Chirathivat, has two children.

Personal Finance
Home Prices Rose 11.3% in 2013; Price Gains Should Moderate as Recovery Ages
620 words
1 March 2014
15:26
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

Home prices last year posted their largest annual gain since 2005, according to the widely watched S&P/Case-Shiller price index, amid a frenzy of sales, low mortgage rates and reduced inventories during the year's first half.

U.S. home prices rose a year-over-year 11.3% in the fourth quarter, the index showed. A subindex measuring prices in 20 major metro areas rose 13.4%.

But there are signs that price gains will soon moderate. "Gains are slowing from month to month, and the strongest part of the recovery in home values may be over," said David Blitzer, chairman of the index committee at S&P Dow Jones Indices.

Indeed, a report by the Demand Institute projected home prices in 2,200 towns and cities surveyed will rise by 2.1% annually between 2015 and 2018.

The gains will vary sharply by location. The report found that the wealthiest 10% of cities accounted for 52% of the country's total housing wealth, while the poorest 40% held just 8%. Since 2000, the value of housing for the top decile rose 73%—around $2 trillion in nominal dollars—while the bottom 40% rose by 59%, or $260 billion.

After falling nearly 35% between 2006 and 2012, home prices have risen 21% since hitting a bottom in early 2012. The price indexes have returned to 2004 levels.

Federal authorities are stepping up efforts to stop what they call a growing problem of fraudulent filings seeking tax refunds based on stolen identities.

On Feb. 22, two members of an alleged crack-dealing gang in Miami were indicted on charges they also ran a tax-refund scam. Suspects typically steal lists of names and Social Security numbers, file large numbers of electronic returns claiming refunds, and receive money before officials spot the fraud.

Identity fraud is partly an outgrowth of electronic filing—in many cases fraudsters take advantage of that and of separate Internal Revenue Service measures intended to ease the filing process for people without bank accounts.

On Monday, the Justice Department said it had filed charges against more than 880 people suspected of stolen-identity tax-refund crimes in the year through Oct. 31. From 2008 through May 2012, the Internal Revenue Service identified more than 550,000 taxpayers whose identities were stolen for the purpose of claiming false refunds in their names, the Justice Department said.

The number of IRS investigations jumped 66% in the past year. The agency initiated 1,492 probes of identity-theft-related crimes in fiscal 2013, up from 898 in 2012 and 276 in 2011.

Local and state governments across the country are tapping the brakes on red-light cameras. After a decade of steady growth, the number of communities using cameras to catch drivers who run stoplights has fallen about 6% since 2012, to 508, according to the Insurance Institute for Highway Safety, a nonprofit group funded by the automobile-insurance industry.

Seven states ban them altogether, says the National Conference of State Legislatures, and several more, including Ohio and Florida, are considering such prohibitions.

Studies of whether the cameras, which are typically mounted on fixtures beside the road, improve traffic safety are numerous and contradictory. Still, many locations, including some big cities, have no plans to get rid of them.

Netflix agreed to pay Comcast to ensure Netflix movies and television shows stream smoothly to Comcast customers. The landmark pact could set a precedent for Netflix's dealings with other broadband providers.
